Ruling on Taking Tithe from Wine but Not Pig
Explains the ruling and reasoning for taking tithe on wine but not pig

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking tithe from wine and pig, explain the two rationales (value substitution and protection), and apply the ruling to analogous cases such as carrion.

Core Idea
Tithe is taken from the value of wine, not pig, due to value substitution and protection principles.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a nuanced Hanafi ruling: the tithe collector takes value from a dhimmi's or harbi's wine but not from pig. The reasoning involves two key Hanafi concepts: the distinction between dhawat al-qiyam (items valued by estimation) and dhawat al-amthal (fungible items), and the principle of protection (himaya). Understanding this ruling deepens your grasp of zakat/tithe rules for non-Muslim traders.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being taken from whom? The tithe collector takes half-tenth of the value of wine from a dhimmi and one-tenth from a harbi, but nothing from pig.
Tithe is taken from value, not the actual wine, because Muslims may not handle wine.
2
Locate the proof
The proof is the statement of Umar (ra): 'If they intend to sell, take the tithe from their prices.' This indicates valuation.
Also relies on istihsan (juristic preference) and qiyas khafi.
3
Analyze the first rationale: value substitution
In dhawat al-qiyam (like pig), value substitutes for the essence. In dhawat al-amthal (like wine), value does not normally substitute, but here an exception is made because taking actual wine is prohibited.
Thus pig is not subject to value substitution, but wine is (by exception).
4
Analyze the second rationale: protection (himaya)
A Muslim protects his own wine for making vinegar, so he protects others' wine. But a Muslim must set his pig free upon embracing Islam, so he does not protect pig.
Hence tithe is taken on wine (to protect the value) but not on pig.
5
Note the application conditions
Conditions: the trader is dhimmi or harbi, passing with goods for trade, and the goods reach nisab (200 dirhams). Valuation by two repentant fasiqs or dhimmis who became Muslim.
No tithe from a Muslim passing with wine by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وعشر الخمر لا الخنزير) أي أخذ نصف عشر قيمة الخمر من الذمي وعشر قيمته من الحربي لا أنه يؤخذ العشر بتمامه منهما، ولأن المأخوذ من عين الخمر لأن المسلم منهي عن اقترابها. ووجه الفرق بين الخمر والخنزير على الظاهر أن القيمة في ذوات القيم لها حكم العين والخنزير منها، وفي ذوات الأمثال ليس لها هذا الحكم والخمر منها ، ولأن حق الأخذ منها للحماية والمسلم يحمي خمر نفسه للتخليل فكذا يحميها على غيره ولا يحمي خنزير نفسه بل يجب تسييبه بالإسلام فكذا لا يحميه على غيره، وسيأتي في آخر باب المهر ما أورد على التعليل الأول وجوابه. وفي الغاية : تعريف قيمة الخمر بقول فاسقين تابا أو ذميين أسلما. وفي الكافي : يعرف ذلك بالرجوع إلى أهل الذمة ا هـ . قيدنا بخمر الذمي والحربي لأن العاشر لا يأخذ من المسلم إذا مر بالخمر اتفاقاً. كذا في الفوائد. وقيد المسألة في المبسوط والأقطع بأن يمر الذمي بالخمر والخنزير للتجارة ويشهد له قول عمر : ولو هم بيعها وخذوا العشر من أثمانها . وفي المعراج قوله مر ذمي بخمر و خنزير أي مر بهما بنية التجارة وهما يساويان مائتي درهم لما ذكرنا من رعاية الشروط في حقه ا هـ. الميتة كالخمر فإنه كان مالاً في الابتداء ويصير مالاً في الانتهاء بالدبغ
His statement: 'And the tithe of wine, not the pig.' That is, one takes half of one-tenth of the value of wine from a dhimmi and one-tenth of its value from a harbi, not that one takes the full tenth from both. And because the thing taken is from the essence of wine, since a Muslim is prohibited from approaching it. And the face of the distinction between wine and pig according to the apparent view is that value in things of value has the ruling of the essence, and the pig is from them, but in things of like-kind this ruling does not apply, and wine is from them. And because the right to take is for protection, and a Muslim protects his own wine for making vinegar, so likewise he protects it from others, but he does not protect his own pig; rather, it must be set loose by Islam, so likewise he does not protect it from others. And it will come at the end of the chapter of Mahr what was objected to the first rationale and its answer. And in al-Ghaya: definition of the value of wine by the statement of two wicked persons who have repented, or two dhimmis who have become Muslim. And in al-Kafi: that is known by reference to the people of dhimma. end. We restricted it to wine of the dhimmi and harbi because the tithe-collector does not take from a Muslim if he passes with wine, by agreement. Similarly in al-Fawaid. And the case is restricted in al-Mabsut and al-Aqta' to the dhimmi passing with wine and pig for trade, and it is supported by the statement of Umar: 'If they intend to sell them, take the tithe from their prices.' And in al-Mi'raj: his statement: 'a dhimmi passed with wine and pig' meaning he passed with them intending trade, and they were equal to two hundred dirhams, because of what we mentioned regarding observing the conditions in his right. end. Carrion is like wine because it was wealth initially and becomes wealth eventually by tanning.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It discusses the ruling on the tithe collector taking from a dhimmi or harbi who passes with wine or pig. The author explains that the tithe collector takes half of one-tenth (i.e., half of the usual 10%) of the value of wine from a dhimmi, and one-tenth from a harbi. This is not taken from the wine itself because Muslims are prohibited from handling it directly. The distinction between wine and pig is based on two reasons: (1) In items that are valued (like pig), value consideration substitutes for the essence, but wine is among items measured by like-kind (like grains) where value does not substitute. (2) The right to take the tithe is for protection of property; a Muslim protects his own wine for eventual vinegar making, so he also protects others' wine, but a Muslim must set his pig free upon embracing Islam, so he does not protect it. The author notes that carrion is treated like wine: it becomes property after tanning. Valuation of wine can be made by the testimony of two formerly sinful persons who repented or by dhimmis who became Muslim, or by consulting dhimmis. The conditions include that the dhimmi passes with the items for trade and that they reach the nisab of 200 dirhams. No tithe is taken from a Muslim passing with wine by consensus. The proof includes the statement of Umar (ra) to take tithe from sale prices. This is a clear Hanafi position relying on istihsan (juristic preference) and qiyas khafi.

Key Terms
العشر (al-'ushr): The tithe taken by the state from traders, usually one-tenth of the value.
ذوات القيم (dhawat al-qiyam): Items that are valued by estimation rather than by measure or weight, such as livestock and manufactured goods.
ذوات الأمثال (dhawat al-amthal): Items that are measured by similar units (like grains, liquids) and are fungible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is the value of wine determined for tithe purposes according to the passage?
A) By the testimony of two just Muslims
B) By the testimony of two repentant fasiqs or dhimmis who embraced Islam
C) By the market price set by a Muslim appraiser
D) By the declaration of the dhimmi owner

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school takes tithe on the value of wine from non-Muslim traders, but not on pig, based on distinctions in value substitution and protection.

Duplicate Tithe When Passing a Kharijite Collector
Ruling with rationale on duplicate tithe

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on duplicate tithe when a trader passes a Kharijite collector, identify the condition of negligence (taqsir), and apply the ruling to analogous scenarios.

Core Idea
Paying an illegitimate authority does not discharge the obligation if the trader was negligent.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical scenario: a trader who has already paid the 'ushr (tithe) to a legitimate collector later passes through territory controlled by the Khawarij and is forced to pay again. Must he pay a second time to the rightful authority? The Hanafi school answers with a clear rule based on the concept of negligence (taqsir). Let's dissect the ruling and its underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is passing a Kharijite tithe collector after already paying the legitimate tithe. The Hanafi ruling is that the trader must pay tithe again (duplicate) to the rightful collector.
2
Locate the rationale (dalil)
The proof is the principle that the Khawarij are illegitimate authorities; their collection is invalid. Additionally, the trader's negligence (taqsir) in choosing to pass through their territory triggers the obligation to repeat the payment.
3
Trace the operative cause (illa)
The operative cause is the trader's negligence. If the trader voluntarily enters Kharijite territory, he is at fault; if the Khawarij have taken over the entire area, the trader has no choice and is not negligent.
Negligence is determined by whether the trader had a practical alternative.
4
Note the exception
If the Khawarij have taken over the entire city or village, the trader is not negligent, and duplicate payment is not required. His first payment to the legitimate authority suffices, and he does not need to repeat.
This exception is based on the absence of taqsir.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, a Hanafi jurist must assess whether the trader had a choice in passing through Kharijite territory. If yes, wajib to pay again; if no, the obligation is already fulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وثنى إن عشر الخوارج) أي أخذ منه ثانياً إن مر على عاشر الخوارج فعشروه لأن التقصير من جهته حيث مر عليهم بخلاف ما إذا ظهروا على مصر أو قرية كما قدمناه
His statement: 'And duplicate, if the Khawarij tithed.' Meaning, it is taken from him a second time if he passes by a tithe collector of the Khawarij and they tithed him, because the negligence is from his side since he passed them, unlike if they had taken over a city or village as we have previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with rationale from the commentary. The subject is when a trader has already paid the 'ushr (tithe) to a legitimate collector but then passes a second collector who is from the Khawarij (outlaws). Must he pay again? Yes, because the Kharijites are not legitimate authorities; their collection is invalid. So the trader must pay a second time to the rightful collector. The reason is the trader's own negligence for passing through territory controlled by the Khawarij. However, if the Khawarij had taken over the entire area (city or village), then it is beyond the trader's control, and he would not be required to pay again. This is a special case where duplicate payment is obligatory due to the illegitimacy of the first collector. The rule is based on taqsir (negligence) from the trader.

Key Terms
الخوارج (al-khawārij): Rebellious group; here, a group that takes tithe illegitimately.
التقصير (al-taqsīr): Negligence or fault on the part of the trader for choosing to pass through Kharijite territory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a trader not required to pay tithe a second time after being tithed by the Khawarij?
A) If the Khawarij were not known as outlaws
B) If the Khawarij had taken over the entire city or village
C) If the trader did not know the first collector was Kharijite
D) If the trader paid voluntarily

Key Takeaway
Duplicate tithe is wajib only when the trader was negligent in choosing to pass through Kharijite territory.

Rukaz and Khums on Minerals
Definition and ruling for khums on minerals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define rukaz in Hanafi fiqh and apply the khums ruling to different types of minerals.

Core Idea
Khums is due on solid smeltable minerals found in any land, but not on liquids or non-smeltable gems.

The chapter on rukaz (buried treasure) opens with a definition and the ruling on khums (one-fifth). The author explains that rukaz includes both human-buried treasure and naturally occurring minerals. Drawing on a hadith and the principle that such wealth resembles booty (ghanimah), the Hanafi school obligates khums on specific minerals. This lesson unpacks that definition, the conditions for khums, and who is liable.

Guided Path
1
Define rukaz linguistically and legally
Identify the root 'rakz' (fixing in the earth) and how it encompasses both kanz (buried treasure) and ma'dan (mineral deposits).
Hanafi school: the term is shared truthfully between both.
2
State the ruling on khums and its proof
The hadith 'In rukaz, the one-fifth' is the primary evidence. The text also draws an analogy to booty, which requires khums.
The hadith is ahad but accepted.
3
Identify the scope of minerals subject to khums
Only solid minerals that are malleable by fire (e.g., gold, silver, iron, lead, copper) are included. Excluded are liquids (tar, naphtha, salt) and non-smeltable solids (gypsum, lime, jewels).
The text uses 'nahw hadid' (similar to iron) as the criterion.
4
Determine the liability of the finder
The finder keeps four-fifths and the treasury takes one-fifth. The rule applies to free and slave, Muslim and dhimmi, adult and child, male and female.
The finder's real hand vs. the state's juridical hand explains the division.
5
Handle the special case of a harbi musta'min
A musta'min (non-Muslim with safe passage) who works a mine without the imam's permission gets nothing; with permission, he receives what was stipulated.
This follows from the booty analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الركاز
قوله: (خمس معدن نقد ونحو حديد في أرض خراج أو عشر ) لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام وفي الركاز الخمس» (۱) وهو من الركز فانطلق على المعدن ولأنه كان في أيدي الكفرة وحوته أيدينا غلبة فكان غنيمة وفي الغنيمة الخمس إلا أن للغانمين يداً حكمية لثبوتها على الظاهر، وأما الحقيقة فللواجد فاعتبرنا الحكمية في حق الخمس والحقيقة في حق الأربعة الأخماس حتى تم للواجد. والنقد الذهب والفضة ونحو الحديد كل جامد ينطبع بالنار كالرصاص والنحاس والصفر، وقيد به احترازاً عن المائعات كالقار والنفط والملح، وعن الجامد الذي لا ينطبع كالجص والنورة والجواهر كالياقوت والفيروزج والزمرد فلا شيء فيها. وأطلق في الواجد فشمل الحر والعبد والمسلم والذمي والبالغ والصبي والذكر والأنثى كما في المحيط . وأما الحربي المستأمن إذا عمل بغير إذن الإمام لم يكن له شيء لأنه لا حق له في الغنيمة، وإن عمل بإذن فله ما شرط
Chapter of Rukaz
His statement: 'One-fifth of a mine of gold and silver and similar to iron on kharaj land or tithe land' due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'And in rukaz, the one-fifth.' It is from 'rakz' so it applies to mines. And because it was in the hands of disbelievers and our hands gained it by overpowering, so it was booty, and in booty one-fifth. Except that the gainers have a juridical hand because of its establishment externally, while the real hand is for the finder. So we considered the juridical hand in respect of the one-fifth and the real hand in respect of the remaining four-fifths so that it is completed for the finder. And 'naqd' (cash) is gold and silver, and 'similar to iron' includes every solid that is malleable by fire, like lead, copper, and brass. It is restricted to exclude liquids like tar, naphtha, and salt, and solids that are not malleable like plaster, lime, and jewels like ruby, turquoise, and emerald, so nothing is on them. And it is absolute regarding the finder, so it includes free and slave, Muslim and dhimmi, adult and child, male and female, as in al-Muhit. As for a harbi musta'min, if he works without the imam's permission, he has nothing because he has no right to the booty; and if he works with permission, he has what was stipulated.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on rukaz (buried treasure/mineral deposits). It first defines rukaz: both treasure (kanz) and mineral (ma'dan) are included, as the term derives from 'rakz' (fixing in the earth). The ruling is that one-fifth (khums) is due on a mine of gold, silver, and similar metals that can be smelted by fire (like iron, lead, copper, brass). This applies regardless of whether the land is kharaj or 'ushr land. The proof is the hadith: 'In rukaz, the one-fifth.' The rationale is that such minerals were formerly in the hands of disbelievers and were taken by force, thus they are like ghanimah (booty), which requires khums. However, the finder has a 'real hand' (control) over the item, while the state has a 'juridical hand' for the khums portion. Thus the finder gets four-fifths and the treasury gets one-fifth. The items subject to khums are restricted to solid, smeltable minerals; liquids (e.g., petroleum) and non-smeltable solids (e.g., gypsum) are exempt. The finder's identity is broad: free, slave, Muslim, dhimmi, adult, child, male, female. However, a musta'min (harbi with safe passage) who works a mine without the imam's permission gets nothing; with permission, he gets what was stipulated. This is the Hanafi position as stated in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Key Terms
الركاز (al-rukāz): Both treasure buried by humans (kanz) and minerals created in the earth (ma'dan).
الخمس (al-khums): A tax of one-fifth on discovered treasures and specific minerals.
ينطبع بالنار (yantabi' bi al-nār): Malleable by fire; capable of being smelted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on which of the following is khums due?
A) A mine of emeralds
B) A spring of petroleum
C) A mine of copper
D) A deposit of table salt

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, khums (one-fifth) is wajib on solid smeltable minerals found on any land, excluding liquids and non-smeltable solids.

The Ruling on Khums for Minerals and Buried Treasure
Ruling with proof on khums and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khums for minerals and rikaz, identify the conditions for its obligation, and apply the ruling to scenarios involving owned and non-owned land.

Core Idea
Khums (one-fifth) is wajib on minerals and treasure found in non-owned land of Dar al-Islam.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on khums for minerals (ma'din) and buried treasure (rikaz), based on the hadith 'Fi al-rikaz al-khums'. The passage from Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that khums is due on finds in kharaj or 'ushr land—but not from one's own house or land. It also addresses scenarios involving hired workers, selling found rikaz, and the finder's eligibility to consume the khums if needy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage rules that khums (one-fifth) is wajib on minerals and rikaz found in non-owned land. Note that the hukm level is wajib, not fard, because the dalil is a hadith ahad.
The hadith 'Fi al-rikaz al-khums' is the primary proof.
2
Locate the conditions (shurut)
The obligation is conditioned on the find being in kharaj or 'ushr land that is non-owned (not one's own house or land). This excludes owned land and houses, where khums is not obligatory according to Abu Hanifa.
There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad obligate khums even in owned land.
3
Apply the proof (dalil)
The hadith 'Fi al-rikaz al-khums' is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear (qati al-dalalah). The imperative 'al-khums' indicates obligation, but due to zanni transmission, it is wajib, not fard.
The Hanafi usul principle: ahad hadith yields wujub, not fard.
4
Examine the intra-school differences
Abu Hanifa: no khums on finds in one's own house or land. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: khums is obligatory. The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is Abu Hanifa's view, as preferred by al-Marghinani.
Other schools (Shafi'i etc.) obligate khums in all lands, including owned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الزكاة باب الركاز خمس معدن نقد ونحو حديد في أرض خراج أو عشر لا داره وأرضه وكنز استعمله فيه، وإذا عمل رجلان في طلب الركاز وأصابه أحدهما يكون للواحد لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام جعل أربعة أخماسه للواجد . وإذا استأجر أجراء للعمل في المعدن فالمصاب للمستأجر لأنهم يعملون له. وعن أبي يوسف : لو وجد ركازاً فباعه بعوض فالخمس على الذي في يده الركاز ويرجع على البائع بخمس الثمن. كذا في المحيط وفي المبسوط : ومن أصاب ركازاً وسعه أن يتصدق بخمسه على المساكين فإذا اطلع الإمام على ذلك أمضى له ما صنع لأن الخمس حق الفقراء وقد أوصله إلى مستحقه وهو في إصابة الركاز غير محتاج إلى الحماية فهو كزكاة الأموال الباطنة ا هـ . وفي البدائع : ويجوز دفع الخمس إلى الوالدين والمولودين الفقراء كما في الغنائم، ويجوز للواجد أن يصرفه إلى نفسه إذا كان محتاجاً ولا تغنيه الأربعة الأخماس بأن كان دون المائتين، أما إذا بلغ مائتين فإنه لا يجوز له تناول الخمس ا هـ وهو دليل على وجوب الخمس مع فقر الواجد وجواز صرفه لنفسه، ولا يقال ينبغي أن لا يجب الخمس مع الفقر كاللقطة لأنا نقول : إن النص عام فيتناوله . كذا في المعراج. وقيد بكونه في أرض خراج أو عشر ليخرج الدار فإنه لا شيء فيها لكن ورد عليه الأرض التي لا وظيفة فيها كالمفازة إذ يقتضي أنه لا شيء في المأخوذ منها وليس كذلك، فالصواب أن لا يجعل ذلك القصد الاحتراز بل للتنصيص على أن وظيفتهما المستمرة لا تمنع الأخذ مما يوجد فيها. كذا في فتح القدير. وفي المغرب : خُمس القوم إذا أخذ خمس أموالهم من باب طلب ا.هـ. واستشهد له في ضياء الحلوم بقول عدي بن حاتم الطائي : ربعت في الجاهلية وخمست في الإسلام. والخُمس بضمتين وقد تسكن الميم وبه قرئ في قوله تعالى فإن لله خمسه ﴾ [الأنفال : ٤١]١ هـ فعلم أن قوله في المختصر «خمس» بتخفيف الميم لأنه متعد فجاز بناء المفعول منه و به اندفع قول من قرأه خمس - بتشديد الميم - ظناً . منه أن المخفف لازم لما علمت أن المخفف متعد وأنه من باب طلب . في المعدن مجازاً في الكنز تأمل ا.هـ. قلت وفيه نظر ظاهر فتدبر قوله : ( وقيد بكونه في أرض خراج أو عشر الخ) أقول : المفهوم من كلام البدائع أن المراد من أرض الخراج والعشر هو الأرض الغير المملوكة فإنه قال : وأما المعدن فلا يخلو إما إن وجده في دار الإسلام أو دار الحرب في أرض مملوكة أو غير مملوكة، فإن وجده في دار الإسلام في أرض غير مملوكة ففيه الخمس، وإن وجده في أرض مملوكه أو دار أو منزل أو حانوت فلا خلاف في أن الأربعة أخماس لصاحب الملك وحده هو غيره، واختلف في وجوب الخمس ثم قال : وأما إذا وجده في دار الحرب الخ. لكن إذا حمل كلام المصنف هنا على غير المملوكة وذلك كالمفازة يرد عليه أنها ليست عشرية ولا خراجية فكيف يعبر عنها بأرض العشر أو الخراج إلا أن يوجد أرض عشر أو خراج غير مملوكة قوله : (فالصواب أن لا يجعل ذلك لقصد الاحتراز (الخ) قال في النهر فيه بحث بل يصح أن يكون للاحتراز عن الدار ويعلم حكم المفازة بالأولى لأنه إذا وجب في الأرض مع الوظيفة فلأن يجب في الخالية عنها أولى ا أو
Book of Zakat, Chapter of Rikaz. One-fifth of mineral (gold, silver, and the like of iron) in kharaj or 'ushr land – not his house and his land – and treasure found in it. And if two men work seeking rikaz and one of them finds it, it belongs to that one because the Prophet (peace be upon him) made four-fifths for the finder. And if he hires workers to work in the mine, what is found belongs to the employer because they work for him. From Abu Yusuf: If he finds rikaz and sells it for compensation, the khums is on the one in whose hand the rikaz is, and he recovers from the seller one-fifth of the price. Likewise in al-Muhit. And in al-Mabsut: Whoever finds rikaz may give its khums in charity to the poor; if the imam learns of that he validates what he did because khums is the right of the poor and he has delivered it to its rightful recipient, and in finding rikaz he is not in need of protection; it is like the zakat of hidden wealth. And in al-Bada'i: It is permissible to give the khums to poor parents and children as in war booty, and it is permissible for the finder to spend it on himself if he is needy and the four-fifths do not suffice him, i.e., if he owns less than two hundred (dirhams). But if he reaches two hundred, then it is not permissible for him to take the khums. This is evidence for the obligation of khums even if the finder is poor, and the permissibility of spending it on himself. And it is not said that khums should not be obligatory when poor like luqata, because we say: the text is general so it includes him. Likewise in al-Mi'raj. And it is conditioned that it be in kharaj or 'ushr land to exclude the house, for there is nothing in it. But it is objected concerning land that has no function like the desert, for it would imply that there is nothing taken from it, but it is not so. The correct view is that this is not intended for exclusion, but to state that their continuous function does not prevent taking from what is found in them. Likewise in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Mughrib: Khums of the people when he takes one-fifth of their wealth from the category of 'request'. And it is supported in Diya' al-Hulum by the saying of 'Adi ibn Hatim al-Ta'i: I tax-collected in Jahiliyyah and I khums in Islam. And al-khums with two dammas, and the meem may be sukoon, and with it was recited in His statement: 'Then for Allah is one-fifth of it' [Anfal 8:41]. So it is known that his statement in al-Mukhtasar 'khums' with a light meem because it is transitive so the passive participle is permissible from it, and by this is refuted the saying of one who read it 'khummis' with a shaddah on meem, mistakenly thinking that the light form is intransitive, since you have learned that the light form is transitive and it is from the category of 'request'. In the mine figuratively in the treasure, contemplate. End. I say: There is apparent weakness in it, so reflect. His saying: 'And it is conditioned that it be in kharaj or 'ushr land...' I say: What is understood from the speech of al-Bada'i is that by kharaj and 'ushr land is meant non-owned land. He said: As for the mine, it is either found in Dar al-Islam or Dar al-Harb in owned or non-owned land. If found in Dar al-Islam in non-owned land, there is khums in it. If found in owned land or a house or dwelling or shop, there is no disagreement that the four-fifths belong to the owner, but there is disagreement on the obligation of khums. Then he said: And if he finds it in Dar al-Harb... But if we interpret the author's statement here as meaning non-owned land such as the desert, it is objected that it is not 'ushr nor kharaj land, so how can he express it as 'ushr or kharaj land unless there exists 'ushr or kharaj land that is non-owned? His saying: 'The correct view is that this is not intended for exclusion...' said in al-Nahr: There is discussion on it, rather it is valid that it be for exclusion of the house, and the ruling of the desert is known a fortiori, because if it is obligatory in land with a function, then it is more appropriate that it be obligatory in land free from it. End.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof regarding khums (one-fifth) on minerals (ma'din) and buried treasure (rikaz) according to the Hanafi school. The author begins by stating the general rule: khums is due on minerals (whether gold, silver, or iron) found in kharaj or 'ushr land, but not from one's own house or land. Similarly, treasure (kanz) found in such land incurs khums. The hadith 'In rikaz is khums' is the primary proof, though not quoted verbatim here. The text then addresses several scenarios: If two men search for rikaz and only one finds it, the finder gets the four-fifths, and the other gets nothing because the Prophet assigned the four-fifths to the finder. If a person hires workers to work in a mine, whatever they find belongs to the employer because they work for him. A narration from Abu Yusuf adds: if someone sells rikaz that he found, the khums is on the seller (the one who had the rikaz), and he can recover one-fifth of the price from the buyer. The text also discusses payment of khums: the finder may pay it directly to the poor without waiting for the imam; the imam will validate it if he finds out. The finder may also give khums to his poor parents or children, and may even spend it on himself if he is needy and the four-fifths are insufficient, meaning he has less than 200 dirhams. However, if he has 200 dirhams, he cannot take khums for himself. This shows that khums is obligatory even if the finder is poor, and he may use it for his own need. The condition that the mineral be found in kharaj or 'ushr land is explained: it excludes the house, but the desert (mafazah) which has no land tax is included by analogy (qiyas al-awla) because if khums is due in land with tax, it is more due in land without tax. The linguistic discussion clarifies that the word 'khums' in the matn is with a light meem (transitive) meaning 'to take one-fifth', not with shaddah. The commentary from al-Bada'i further clarifies that 'kharaj or 'ushr land' here means non-owned land; in owned land, the four-fifths belong to the owner, but there is disagreement on khums. The author resolves by stating that the condition is for excluding the house, not for limiting to taxed land.

Key Terms
ركاز (rikaz): Buried treasure (defīnah) from pre-Islamic times, or minerals in the earth. Here it is used inclusively for both.
معدن (ma'din): A mine; a place from which minerals (gold, silver, iron, etc.) are extracted.
خمس (khums): One-fifth; the share payable on rikaz and ma'din, distributed to the poor or to Bayt al-Mal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person finds a silver nugget in a non-owned 'ushr land, what must he do?
A) He must pay khums (one-fifth) to the poor or Bayt al-Mal.
B) He may keep it all because it is his find.
C) He must pay 2.5% zakat on the value.
D) He must give it to the imam for distribution.

Key Takeaway
Khums (20%) is wajib on minerals and treasure found in non-owned land of Dar al-Islam; the finder may pay it directly to the poor and may use it himself if needy.

Zakat — Almsgiving

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Khums for Minerals in Owned Property
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khums for minerals in owned property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions regarding khums on minerals found in one's own house or land, explain the proof of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A mineral found in owned land is like the land itself — no khums, because no recurring expense on its parts.

In this lesson, we examine a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: does khums apply to minerals discovered in one's own house or land? All four imams agree on khums for buried treasure, but they differ sharply on minerals. The Hanafi school itself has two camps: Abu Hanifa exempts such minerals, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, obligate khums. We will trace the arguments and see which position is relied upon in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed points
All agree that the four-fifths belong to the landowner, and that khums is obligatory on minerals in non-owned land. The disagreement is only about owned land and houses.
2
State the disagreement
Abu Hanifa says no khums on minerals in one's own house or land; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say khums is wajib due to the general hadith.
Two narrations from Abu Hanifa on land: one like the house (no khums), another like the Sahibayn. The author chooses the first.
3
Analyze each party's proof
Sahibayn cite the general hadith 'Fi al-rikaz al-khums'. Abu Hanifa relies on qiyas: the mineral is part of the land, and since no recurring expense (mu'nah) is on other parts, likewise no khums on this part. Also, owned land bears 'ushr/kharaj, unlike a house.
The distinction between land and house: land has expenses, house does not.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The author prefers Abu Hanifa's view; the mufta bihi is no khums on minerals in one's own house or land, regardless of who finds it. The opposing view is considered but not relied upon.
Fath al-Qadir notes the ikhtilaf but does not preponderate.

قوله : (لا داره وأرضه أي لا خمس في معدن وجده في داره أو أرضه فاتفقوا على أن الأربعة أخماس للمالك، سواء وجده هو أو غيره، لأنه من توابع الأرض بدليل دخوله في البيع بغير تسمية فيكون من أجزائها واختلفوا في وجوب الخمس، قال أبو حنيفة : لا خمس في الدار والبيت والمنزل والحانوت مسلماً كان المالك أو ذمياً كما في المحيط، وفي الأرض عنه روايتان اختار المصنف أنها كالدار وقالا : يجب الخمس لإطلاق الدليل، وله أنه من أجزاء الأرض مركب فيها ولا مؤنة في سائر الأجزاء فكذا في هذا الجزء لأن الجزء لا يخالف الجملة بخلاف الكنز فإنه غير مركب فيها والفرق بين الأرض والدار على إحدى الروايتين . وهي رواية الجامع الصغير أن الدار : هذا كله إذا وجد في دار الإسلام، فأما إذا وجده في دار الحرب فإن وجده في أرض غير مملوكه فهو ولا خمس فيه كما في الكنز وأورد على كون المعدن من أجزاء الأرض جواز التيمم به وليس بجائز، وأجاب في المعراج بأنه من أجزائها وليس من جنسها كالخشب قوله : (وكنز) بالرفع عطف على معدن» أي وخمس كنز وهو دفين الجاهلية فيكون الخمس لبيت المال، وله يصرفه إلى نفسه إن كان فقيراً كما قدمناه ووجوب الخمس اتفاقاً المعدن، في العموم الحديث وفي الركاز الخمس كما قدمناه قوله : ( وباقيه للمختط له) أي الأخماس الأربعة للذي ملكه الإمام البقعة أوّل الفتح وإن كان ميتاً فلورثته إن عرفوا وإلا فهو لأقصى مالك للأرض أو لورثته كذا في البدائع. وقيل: يوضع في بيت المال ورجحه في فتح القدير. أن ( قللت : وفي دعوى الأولوية نظر لأنهم جعلوا عدم لزوم المؤن دليلاً على عدم وجوب الخمس كما يذكره المؤلف في المقولة الآتية تأمل قوله : (لما علمت أن المخفف متعد أي فيبنى للمفعول من غر نقله إلى باب التضعيف على أن التشديد لا معنى له هنا لأن خمست الشيء بمعنى جعلته خمسة أخماس كما في النهر، وأما الذي بمعنى أخذت خمسه فهو المخفف كما مر عن المغرب قوله : (واختلفوا في وجوب الخمس الظاهر أن الخلاف فيه جارٍ في الأرض المملوكة للواجد أو لغيره بدليل قوله قبله تبعاً للبدائع، سواء وجده هو أو غيره أي المالك أو غير المالك، فقول المتن «لا داره وأرضه» بإرجاع الضمير للواجد ليس احترازاً عن الأرض المملوكة لغير الواجد بل هما سواء في عدم وجوب الخمس فيهما كما استويا في أن الأربعة أخماس للمالك سواء كان هو الواجب أوغيره. وعبارة التنوير تقتضي خلاف ذلك فإنه قال : وباقيه أي باقي المعدن بعد الخمس لمالكها إن ملكت وإلا فللواجد ولا شيء فيه إن وجده في داره وأرضه فقوله وباقيه لمالكه يدل على أنه لو كان الواجد غير الممالك يخمس والباقي للمالك، ولو كان الواجد هو المالك لا يخمس بل الكل له لقوله بعده ولا شيء فيه
His saying: 'Not his house and his land' meaning no khums on a mineral found in his house or his land. They agreed that the four-fifths belong to the owner, whether the finder is he or another, because it is a part of the land, evidenced by its inclusion in a sale without specification, so it is among its parts. And they differed on the obligation of khums. Abu Hanifa said: no khums in the house, dwelling, residence, and shop, whether the owner is Muslim or dhimmi, as in al-Muhit. Regarding land, there are two narrations from him; the author chose that it is like the house. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: khums is obligatory due to the generality of the evidence. And for him (Abu Hanifa) it is that it is part of the land, compounded in it, and there is no recurring expense (mu'nah) in the other parts, so also in this part, because the part does not contradict the whole, unlike treasure (kanz) which is not compounded in it. And the difference between land and house according to one narration – which is the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir – is that the house is owned free of expenses, unlike land, hence 'ushr or kharaj is obligatory on land but not on the house; so also this expense. So they said: if in the house there is a palm tree that yields loads of fruit every year, nothing is obligatory on it because we said, unlike land. And in al-Bada'i: All this is if found in Dar al-Islam. As for if he finds it in Dar al-Harb, if he finds it in non-owned land, then there is no khums in it, as in treasure. And it is objected that the permissibility of tayammum with the mineral indicates it is not part of the land, but it is not permissible; and it is answered in al-Mi'raj that it is part of it but not of its genus, like wood. His saying: 'And treasure' (wakanz) with nominative case, coordinated to 'mine', meaning: and one-fifth of treasure, which is pre-Islamic burial, so the khums is for Bayt al-Mal, and he may spend it on himself if he is poor as we mentioned, and the obligation of khums is agreed upon for the mine; in general the hadith 'And in rikaz is khums' as we mentioned. His saying: 'And the rest for the one who is granted (al-mukhtat) meaning the four-fifths for the one to whom the Imam granted the plot at the time of the first conquest, and if he is dead, to his heirs if known; otherwise to the furthest owner of the land or his heirs, as in al-Bada'i. And it is said: it is placed in Bayt al-Mal, and this is preponderated in Fath al-Qadir. I say: And in the claim of priority (al-awlawiyya) there is consideration because they made the absence of recurring expenses a proof for the non-obligation of khums as the author mentions in the following statement. Contemplate. His saying: 'Since you have learned that the light form is transitive' meaning it is built for the passive from… he transferred it to the pattern of tashdiid, and the tashdiid has no meaning here because 'khammastu al-shay'a' means I made it five parts, as in al-Nahr. As for that which means 'I took one-fifth of it', it is the light form as past in al-Mughrib. His saying: 'And they differed on the obligation of khums' – apparent is that the disagreement applies to land owned by the finder or another, as indicated by his previous statement following al-Bada'i: 'whether he or another finds it' i.e., the owner or non-owner. So the statement of the matn 'not his house and his land' with the pronoun referring to the finder is not an exclusion of land owned by a non-finder; rather they are equal in non-obligation of khums as they are equal in that the four-fifths belong to the owner whether the one who finds is he or another. The expression of al-Tanwir suggests otherwise, for it said: 'And the rest, i.e., the rest of the mine after khums, belongs to its owner if it is owned; otherwise to the finder, and there is nothing in it if he finds it in his house and land.' So his saying 'and the rest to its owner' indicates that if the finder is not the owner, khums is taken and the rest goes to the owner; and if the finder is the owner, he does not pay khums but takes all, because of his subsequent statement 'and nothing in it'.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding the obligation of khums on minerals found in one's own house or land. The text begins by explaining the matn phrase 'لا داره وأرضه' (not his house and his land), meaning there is no khums on a mineral found in one's own house or land. All agree that the four-fifths belong to the owner of the land, whether the finder is the owner or someone else, because the mineral is considered a part of the land (as it is included in a sale without specification). The disagreement is on the obligation of khums (the one-fifth). Abu Hanifa holds that no khums is due on minerals found in one's house, dwelling, residence, or shop, whether the owner is Muslim or dhimmi. Regarding land, there are two narrations from him; the author of the matn (al-Tanwir/ al-Durr?) chose that it is like the house (i.e., no khums). However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) say khums is obligatory due to the generality of the evidence (the hadith 'Fi al-rikaz al-khums' applies to all minerals). Abu Hanifa's argument is that the mineral is an integral part of the land, compounded in it, and since there is no recurring expense (mu'nah) on the other parts of the land (like soil), likewise no khums is due on this part. He distinguishes between a mineral (which is part of the land) and buried treasure (kanz) which is not part of the land, hence khums applies to kanz. A further distinction is made between land and a house: according to one narration from al-Jami' al-Saghir, a house is owned free of expenses, whereas land is subject to 'ushr or kharaj, so these expenses are analogous to khums. Thus, if there were a palm tree in a house yielding fruit, no khums would be due, unlike land. The passage also discusses the ruling in Dar al-Harb: if found in non-owned land, there is no khums, akin to treasure. An objection is raised: if the mineral is part of the land, then tayammum with it should be permissible, but it is not. The answer is that it is part of the land but not of its genus (like wood). The text then addresses the term 'kanz' (treasure) and its khums, which is agreed upon. The distribution of the remaining four-fifths is for the person to whom the Imam granted the land (al-mukhtat) or his heirs; if unknown, it goes to Bayt al-Mal. Finally, the commentator resolves the apparent contradiction in the matn: the phrase 'not his house and his land' applies equally whether the finder is the owner or another; in both cases, no khums is due on the mineral (according to Abu Hanifa's view). The opposing view from al-Tanwir is noted, which limits the non-obligation to when the finder is the owner, but the preferred Hanafi position is that khums is not due on owned land regardless of who finds it.

Key Terms
دار (dar): A house or dwelling place; in this context, the built property owned by a person.
أرض مملوكة (ard mamlukah): Owned land; land that is private property, whether the finder is the owner or not.
مختط له (mukhtat lahu): The person to whom the Imam grants a plot of land (iqta') at the time of conquest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The finder pays khums.
B) The landowner pays khums.
C) No khums is due.
D) The finder and owner split khums.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, minerals found in one's own house or land are not subject to khums, based on the principle that the mineral is part of the land and the landowner already bears expenses.

Khums on a Mine in One's House or Land: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khums for mines

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on khums for a mine in a house versus land and apply the tarjih mechanism to determine the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
The mine follows its location: no khums in the house, but a split among Hanafi imams on land.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a key intra-Hanafi dispute: when you find a mine on your property, must you pay khums (one-fifth) to the state? Abu Hanifa holds that no khums is due if the mine is in your house, and the same applies to land in his preferred view. The Sahibayn disagree, arguing the general evidence requires khums. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to trace how Hanafi jurists reason from the nature of property and prior charges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed ruling
First, note what is uncontested: the four-fifths of the mine belong to the owner regardless of who found it. The dispute is only about the remaining one-fifth (khums).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa says no khums in a house (dar), dwelling, or shop. For land (ard), there are two narrations; the chosen view (mukhtar) is that land is like the house, so no khums.
The author of al-Hidayah, al-Marghinani, prefers this narration.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that khums is wajib in both house and land, citing the general evidence of the hadith 'fi al-rikaz al-khums'.
They consider the mine analogous to treasure (kanz).
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's dalil
His reasoning: the mine is part of the land's components. Since no charge (mu'nah) applies to other parts of the land, it should not apply to this part either. The part does not differ from the whole.
Distinction from kanz: treasure is not composed in the land, it is separate.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: according to the mufta bihi (Abu Hanifa's view), the owner keeps all of the mine. According to the Sahibayn, he must pay khums on the entire find.
This affects whether zakat or khums is due; in practice, the mufta bihi relieves the owner of khums on a mine in his own property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا داره وأرضه أي لا خمس في معدن وجده في داره أو أرضه فاتفقوا على أن الأربعة الأخماس للمالك، سواء وجده هو أو غيره، لأنه من توابع الأرض بدليل دخوله في البيع بغير تسمية فيكون من أجزائها واختلفوا في وجوب الخمس، قال أبو حنيفة : لا خمس في الدار والبيت والمنزل والحانوت مسلماً كان المالك أو ذمياً كما في المحيط، وفي الأرض عنه روايتان اختار المصنف أنها كالدار وقالا : يجب الخمس لإطلاق الدليل، وله أنه من أجزاء الأرض مركب فيها ولا مؤنة في سائر الأجزاء فكذا في هذا الجزء لأن الجزء لا يخالف الجملة بخلاف الكنز فإنه غير مركب فيها والفرق بين الأرض والدار على إحدى الروايتين . وهي رواية الجامع الصغير أن الدار ملكت خالية عن المؤن دون الأرض ولذا وجب العشر أو الخراج في الأرض دون الدار فكذا هذه المؤنة حتى قالوا لو كان في الدار نخلة تطرح كل سنة أكراراً من الثمار لا يجب فيه شيء لما قلنا بخلاف الأرض. وفي البدائع : هذا كله إذا وجد في دار الإسلام، فأما إذا وجده في دار الحرب فإن وجده في أرض غير مملوكه فهو ولا خمس فيه كما في الكنز وأورد على كون المعدن من أجزاء الأرض جواز التيمم به وليس بجائز، وأجاب في المعراج بأنه من أجزائها وليس من جنسها كالخشب
His statement: (not his house and his land) means there is no khums in a mine found in his house or his land. They agreed that the four-fifths are for the owner, whether he found it or someone else, because it is from the appurtenances of the land, evidenced by its inclusion in a sale without specification, thus it is part of it. And they differed on the obligation of khums. Abu Hanifa said: no khums in the house, the dwelling, the home, and the shop, whether the owner is Muslim or dhimmi, as in al-Muhit. Regarding land, there are two narrations from him; the author chose that it is like the house. And they (the Sahibayn) said: khums is obligatory due to the general evidence. And for him (Abu Hanifa) that it is from the parts of the land, composed in it, and there is no charge in the other parts, so likewise in this part because the part does not differ from the whole, unlike the treasure (kanz) for it is not composed therein. And the difference between land and house according to one of the two narrations is the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir that the house was owned free of charges while the land was not, hence 'ushr or kharaj is obligatory on land but not on the house, so likewise this charge. Until they said: if there is a palm tree in the house that produces many loads of fruit every year, nothing is obligatory on it because of what we said, unlike land. And in al-Bada'i': all this is if found in Dar al-Islam. As for if he finds it in Dar al-Harb, if he finds it in land not owned, then there is no khums on it as in the treasure. And an objection was raised against the mine being part of the land that tayammum is permissible with it, but it is not permissible. And he answered in al-Mi'raj that it is a part of it but not of its genus, like wood.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's phrase 'لا داره وأرضه' (not his house and land). It opens by stating the agreed ruling: the four-fifths of a mine found in one's house or land belong to the owner. Then it presents the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding khums (one-fifth). Abu Hanifa holds that no khums is due on a mine found in a house (including similar structures like a shop) regardless of the owner's religion. For land, there are two narrations; the preferred view (mukhtar) is that it is like the house. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that khums is wajib due to the general evidence (the hadith 'fi al-rikaz al-khums' which includes mines). Abu Hanifa's proof is that the mine is an integral part of the land, and since no charges (mu'nah) apply to other parts (like soil), none apply to this part either. The distinction between house (dar) and land (ard) is based on the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir: the house was owned free of external charges (like 'ushr or kharaj), whereas land is subject to such charges, so the mine in land may also be subject to khums analogously. The passage then notes that in Dar al-Harb, if the mine is found in unowned land, no khums is due. Finally, it addresses an objection: if the mine is part of the land, why can't tayammum be performed with it? The answer is that it is a part but not of the same genus, analogous to wood being part of a tree but not valid for tayammum.

Key Terms
معدن (ma'din): A mine or mineral deposit found in the earth.
خمس (khums): One-fifth tax on rikaz and treasure.
دار (dar): House or dwelling, including attached structures.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Khums is wajib because of the general hadith.
B) No khums is due because land is treated like the house according to the chosen narration.
C) Khums is wajib only if the land is subject to kharaj.
D) The ruling depends on whether the mine was discovered by the owner or someone else.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi holds that no khums is due on a mine found in one's own house or land, because the mine is part of the property and no charges apply to such property.

Ruling on Treasure (Kanz) and Its Four-Fifths
Commentary on the ruling of kanz and the destination of its four-fifths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on jahili treasure (kanz) by identifying the recipient of the four-fifths and the basis for the khums obligation.

Core Idea
For a jahili treasure, the khums goes to the public treasury, the four-fifths to the land's allotee.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author clarifies two aspects of jahili treasure: the obligation of khums (one-fifth) and the destination of the remaining four-fifths. While the obligation is established by a clear hadith, the allocation of the four-fifths involves an intra-Hanafi dispute that hinges on land ownership. This lesson will guide you through the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Define kanz as jahili buried treasure. The khums (one-fifth) is wajib by hadith.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith 'في الركاز الخمس' (in buried treasure, the khums) is the primary proof.
The hadith is ahad, zanni al-thubut, but dalała is qati for obligation.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The operative word 'al-rikaz' includes kanz (jahili treasure), and 'al-khums' indicates a specific obligatory share.
4
Determine the destination of the four-fifths
The passage states 'و باقيه للمختط له' meaning the four-fifths go to the person to whom the Imam allotted the land during conquest.
If the allotee is deceased, it goes to his heirs; otherwise to the furthest owner.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There is a view that the four-fifths go to bayt al-mal, but the author preponderates the allotee view per Fath al-Qadir.
The author critiques the alternative reasoning in his personal comment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وكنز) بالرفع عطف على معدن» أي وخمس كنز وهو دفين الجاهلية فيكون الخمس لبيت المال، وله يصرفه إلى نفسه إن كان فقيراً كما قدمناه ووجوب الخمس اتفاقاً المعدن، في العموم الحديث وفي الركاز الخمس كما قدمناه قوله : ( وباقيه للمختط له) أي الأخماس الأربعة للذي ملكه الإمام البقعة أوّل الفتح وإن كان ميتاً فلورثته إن عرفوا وإلا فهو لأقصى مالك للأرض أو لورثته كذا في البدائع. وقيل: يوضع في بيت المال ورجحه في فتح القدير. أن ( قلت : وفي دعوى الأولوية نظر لأنهم جعلوا عدم لزوم المؤن دليلاً على عدم وجوب الخمس كما يذكره المؤلف في المقولة الآتية تأمل
His statement: (and treasure) in the nominative is conjuncted to 'mine' meaning and the khums of treasure, which is a jahili burial, so the khums goes to the public treasury, and he (the caliph) may spend it on himself if he is poor, as we have mentioned. The obligation of khums is agreed upon for the mine? No, the general hadith says 'and in rikaz the khums' as we have mentioned. His statement: (and the rest of it is for the person allotted the land) meaning the four-fifths are for the one to whom the Imam gave the land at the time of the first conquest. If he is dead, then for his heirs if known; otherwise it is for the farthest owner of the land or his heirs, as in al-Bada'i'. And it is said: it is placed in the public treasury, and this was preponderated in Fath al-Qadir. I say: And in claiming the first view there is consideration because they made the non-liability for charges a proof for non-obligation of khums, as the author will mention in the upcoming statement, reflect.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage comments on two matn phrases: 'وكنز' (and treasure) and 'وباقيه للمختط له' (and its remainder is for the one allotted the land). First, it defines kanz as a jahili (pre-Islamic) burial, indicating it is not Islamic (otherwise it is luqatah). The khums (one-fifth) of such treasure goes to the public treasury (bayt al-mal). The ruling that the khums is obligatory is established by the hadith 'fi al-rikaz al-khums' (in buried treasure, khums). The four-fifths go to the mukhtatt lahu, which refers to the person to whom the Imam (caliph) allotted that land during the initial conquest (when dividing conquered lands). This is because the land with the treasure is considered owned by that allotee. If the allotee is deceased, it goes to his heirs if known; otherwise to the furthest known owner of the land. There is also a view that the four-fifths go to the bayt al-mal, but the author preponderates (in Fath al-Qadir) the view that they go to the mukhtatt lahu. The author then inserts a personal comment (qultu) noting that the reasoning that no charges (mu'nah) leads to no khums is questionable, referencing the earlier discussion on ma'din.

Key Terms
كنز (kanz): Jahili (pre-Islamic) buried treasure.
مختط له (mukhtatt lahu): A person to whom the Imam allots a piece of land during the initial conquest for building or cultivation.
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Muslim state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The finder always.
B) The public treasury.
C) The person to whom the land was allotted by the Imam (mukhtatt lahu) or his heirs.
D) The current owner of the land.

Key Takeaway
The khums on jahili treasure is wajib, and the remaining four-fifths are for the one to whom the land was allotted by the Imam.

The Correct Verb Form for Taking One-Fifth
Grammatical clarification on the verb 'خمس'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the lightened (mukhaffaf) and intensified (mushaddad) forms of the verb 'خمس' and state that the fiqh term 'khums' (one-fifth) is derived from the lightened form meaning 'to take one-fifth'.

Core Idea
The verb for 'taking one-fifth' is the lightened form, while the intensified form means 'to divide into five-fifths'.

In Arabic grammar, a single root can yield different meanings when verb patterns change. The verb 'خمس' appears in two forms: with tashdid (intensified) and without (lightened). The commentator clarifies that the correct form for the fiqh concept of khums is the lightened form, meaning 'to take the fifth part'. This linguistic precision avoids misinterpretation of legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two verb forms
Recognize that the verb 'خمس' can be either 'khammasa' (with tashdid) or 'khamasa' (without tashdid) in the past tense. The first is intensified, the second lightened.
In Arabic, pattern Fa''ala often indicates causation or intensification.
2
Distinguish the meanings
According to the commentary, 'khammastu al-shay'' (intensified) means 'I made it into five-fifths'. 'Khamastu al-shay'' (lightened) means 'I took its fifth'.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the term 'خمس' in fiqh contexts.
3
Apply to the matn
When the matn or the commentary uses 'خمس' in the context of mines, it is the lightened form that is intended, meaning the obligation to pay one-fifth.
The intensified form would imply a different ruling altogether, which is not relevant here.
4
Verify with references
The commentator cites al-Nahr and al-Mughrib as sources for this linguistic usage. Students should note these as authoritative linguistic references within the Hanafi school.
Al-Mughrib is a lexicon by al-Mutarrizi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لما علمت أن المخفف متعد أي فيبنى للمفعول من غر نقله إلى باب التضعيف على أن التشديد لا معنى له هنا لأن خمست الشيء بمعنى جعلته خمسة أخماس كما في النهر، وأما الذي بمعنى أخذت خمسه فهو المخفف كما مر عن المغرب قوله : (واختلفوا في وجوب الخمس الظاهر أن الخلاف فيه جارٍ في الأرض المملوكة للواجد أو لغيره بدليل قوله قبله تبعاً للبدائع، سواء وجده هو أو غيره أي المالك أو غير المالك، فقول المتن «لا داره وأرضه» بإرجاع الضمير للواجد ليس احترازاً عن الأرض المملوكة لغير الواجد بل هما سواء في عدم وجوب الخمس فيهما كما استويا في أن الأربعة الأخماس للمالك سواء كان هو الواجب أوغيره. وعبارة التنوير تقتضي خلاف ذلك فإنه قال : وباقيه أي باقي المعدن بعد الخمس لمالكها إن ملكت وإلا فللواجد ولا شيء فيه إن وجده في داره وأرضه فقوله وباقيه لمالكه يدل على أنه لو كان الواجد غير الممالك يخمس والباقي للمالك، ولو كان الواجد هو المالك لا يخمس بل الكل له لقوله بعده ولا شيء فيه
His statement: (Since you know that the lightened form is transitive) meaning it is passive from 'gharra'? He transferred it to the intensified form, that the intensification has no meaning here because 'khammastu al-shay'' means I made it five-fifths, as in al-Nahr. As for the meaning of 'I took its fifth', it is the lightened form as passed from al-Mughrib. His statement: (And they differed on the obligation of khums) Apparently the dispute runs in land owned by the finder or other, as evidenced by his statement before, following al-Bada'i': whether he found it himself or another, i.e., the owner or non-owner. So the matn's statement 'his house and his land' with the pronoun referring to the finder is not a restriction to land owned by the finder, rather both are equal in the non-obligation of khums, just as they are equal in the four-fifths belonging to the owner whether he is the finder or not. The expression of al-Tanwir suggests otherwise, for he said: 'and its remainder i.e., the remainder of the mine after khums is for its owner if it is owned, otherwise for the finder, and there is nothing in it if found in his house and his land.' So his statement 'and its remainder is for its owner' indicates that if the finder is not the owner, khums is paid and the remainder goes to the owner; and if the finder is the owner, he does not pay khums, rather all is for him, because of his statement after that 'and nothing in it'.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage provides two clarifications. First, a grammatical note on the verb 'خمس' (khammasa). The commentator explains that 'خمست' with tashdid (doubled letter) means 'I made it into five-fifths', while 'خمست' without tashdid (lightened) means 'I took its fifth'. Therefore, in the phrase 'خمس الكنز', the proper form is the lightened verb meaning 'take the fifth', not the intensified. This is a technical refinement for understanding the matn. Second, the commentator resolves an ambiguity about the scope of the dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding khums on a mine. The text clarifies that the matn's phrase 'لا داره وأرضه' (not his house and his land) uses the pronoun 'his' referring to the finder, but this does not mean the ruling is restricted to land owned by the finder. Rather, the dispute applies to both cases: when the finder is the owner and when he is not. In both, according to the chosen view, no khums is due, and the four-fifths go to the owner of the land. The commentator contrasts this with the view in al-Tanwir, which seems to imply that if the finder is not the owner, khums is paid, and the remainder goes to the owner. But the author of the present commentary (Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) rejects that interpretation, insisting that the ruling is consistent: no khums whether the finder is owner or not, and the owner gets the four-fifths.

Key Terms
مخفف (mukhaffaf): The lightened form of the verb without tashdid.
مشدد (mushaddad): The intensified form with tashdid, meaning 'to make into five-fifths'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The intensified form (with tashdid)
B) The lightened form (without tashdid)
C) Both forms are used interchangeably
D) Neither form; a different verb is used

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the correct verb for taking one-fifth is the lightened form 'khamasa', not the intensified 'khammasa'.

Clarifying the Scope of the Dispute on Khums for Mines
Clarification of the scope of the dispute on khums for mines

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the Hanafi ruling on khums for mines applies regardless of whether the finder owns the land, and you will be able to evaluate the interpretation of al-Tanwir that suggests otherwise.

Core Idea
The dispute on khums includes both finder-owned and other-owned land; the matn's pronoun is not a restriction.

The matn states 'لا داره وأرضه' meaning no khums on mines found in one's own house or land. A reader might think this ruling only applies if the finder is the owner. The commentator clarifies that the ruling extends to all land, regardless of ownership. He also warns against the interpretation found in al-Tanwir which seems to differentiate between owner and non-owner finders.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn's statement
The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq says: 'لا داره وأرضه' (not his house and his land). This refers to mines found on the finder's own property.
The pronoun 'his' refers to the finder.
2
Understand the initial possible interpretation
One might think the phrase restricts the ruling: only when the finder owns the land is there no khums; otherwise, khums is due.
This would imply khums is wajib when the finder is not the owner.
3
Apply the commentator's clarification
The commentator (Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) says the pronoun is not a restriction. The ruling 'no khums' applies equally whether the finder owns the land or not. In both cases, the four-fifths belong to the landowner.
The commentator cites al-Bada'i' as support.
4
Contrast with al-Tanwir's interpretation
Al-Tanwir (Tanwir al-Absar) seems to say: if the finder is not the owner, khums is taken and the remainder goes to the owner; if the finder is the owner, no khums. The commentator rejects this, insisting on uniform treatment.
This illustrates a divergence within the Hanafi school on the scope of the ruling.
5
Derive the practical ruling
According to the relied-upon view (mufta bihi), a mine found on any land triggers no khums; instead, the entire mine (or its value) goes to the landowner, and the finder gets nothing? Actually the passage says four-fifths go to the owner; it doesn't specify the finder's share. But the key is no khums is taken.
This is consistent with the classical Hanafi position that mines are not subject to khums (unlike rikaz).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لما علمت أن المخفف متعد أي فيبنى للمفعول من غر نقله إلى باب التضعيف على أن التشديد لا معنى له هنا لأن خمست الشيء بمعنى جعلته خمسة أخماس كما في النهر، وأما الذي بمعنى أخذت خمسه فهو المخفف كما مر عن المغرب قوله : (واختلفوا في وجوب الخمس الظاهر أن الخلاف فيه جارٍ في الأرض المملوكة للواجد أو لغيره بدليل قوله قبله تبعاً للبدائع، سواء وجده هو أو غيره أي المالك أو غير المالك، فقول المتن «لا داره وأرضه» بإرجاع الضمير للواجد ليس احترازاً عن الأرض المملوكة لغير الواجد بل هما سواء في عدم وجوب الخمس فيهما كما استويا في أن الأربعة الأخماس للمالك سواء كان هو الواجب أوغيره. وعبارة التنوير تقتضي خلاف ذلك فإنه قال : وباقيه أي باقي المعدن بعد الخمس لمالكها إن ملكت وإلا فللواجد ولا شيء فيه إن وجده في داره وأرضه فقوله وباقيه لمالكه يدل على أنه لو كان الواجد غير الممالك يخمس والباقي للمالك، ولو كان الواجد هو المالك لا يخمس بل الكل له لقوله بعده ولا شيء فيه
His statement: (Since you know that the lightened form is transitive) meaning it is passive from 'gharra'? He transferred it to the intensified form, that the intensification has no meaning here because 'khammastu al-shay'' means I made it five-fifths, as in al-Nahr. As for the meaning of 'I took its fifth', it is the lightened form as passed from al-Mughrib. His statement: (And they differed on the obligation of khums) Apparently the dispute runs in land owned by the finder or other, as evidenced by his statement before, following al-Bada'i': whether he found it himself or another, i.e., the owner or non-owner. So the matn's statement 'his house and his land' with the pronoun referring to the finder is not a restriction to land owned by the finder, rather both are equal in the non-obligation of khums, just as they are equal in the four-fifths belonging to the owner whether he is the finder or not. The expression of al-Tanwir suggests otherwise, for he said: 'and its remainder i.e., the remainder of the mine after khums is for its owner if it is owned, otherwise for the finder, and there is nothing in it if found in his house and his land.' So his statement 'and its remainder is for its owner' indicates that if the finder is not the owner, khums is paid and the remainder goes to the owner; and if the finder is the owner, he does not pay khums, rather all is for him, because of his statement after that 'and nothing in it'.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage provides two clarifications. First, a grammatical note on the verb 'خمس' (khammasa). The commentator explains that 'خمست' with tashdid (doubled letter) means 'I made it into five-fifths', while 'خمست' without tashdid (lightened) means 'I took its fifth'. Therefore, in the phrase 'خمس الكنز', the proper form is the lightened verb meaning 'take the fifth', not the intensified. This is a technical refinement for understanding the matn. Second, the commentator resolves an ambiguity about the scope of the dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding khums on a mine. The text clarifies that the matn's phrase 'لا داره وأرضه' (not his house and his land) uses the pronoun 'his' referring to the finder, but this does not mean the ruling is restricted to land owned by the finder. Rather, the dispute applies to both cases: when the finder is the owner and when he is not. In both, according to the chosen view, no khums is due, and the four-fifths go to the owner of the land. The commentator contrasts this with the view in al-Tanwir, which seems to imply that if the finder is not the owner, khums is paid, and the remainder goes to the owner. But the author of the present commentary (Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) rejects that interpretation, insisting that the ruling is consistent: no khums whether the finder is owner or not, and the owner gets the four-fifths.

Key Terms
التنوير (al-Tanwir): A later Hanafi text (Tanwir al-Absar) that presents a differing interpretation on khums for mines.
البدائع (al-Bada'i'): Al-Bada'i' al-Sana'i' by al-Kasani, a major Hanafi fiqh work, used here to support the commentator's view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the ruling on khums for a mine found on land owned by someone other than the finder?
A) Khums is wajib because the land is not the finder's.
B) Khums is not wajib; the mine belongs to the landowner.
C) Khums is wajib if the landowner is a Muslim.
D) The finder gets the entire mine.

Key Takeaway
The matn's phrase 'لا داره وأرضه' does not restrict the ruling; khums is not wajib on mines irrespective of land ownership.

The Dispute on Treasure in Owned Land: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on treasure allocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority position on the allocation of a jahili treasure found in owned land, explain the reasoning of the Sahibayn vs. Abu Yusuf, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute.

Core Idea
The hand of the land's allottee precedes—ownership of land extends to subterranean treasures.

When a pre-Islamic treasure is discovered on land that was allotted by the Imam, who is entitled to the four-fifths after khums? The majority of the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that it goes to the person to whom the land was allotted (mukhtatt lahu). Abu Yusuf, however, argues that the finder deserves it. This lesson dissects their proofs, the analogies they use, and the circumstances that affect the ruling, providing a clear framework for applying this nuanced fiqh issue.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the Treasure Ruling
Note that khums (one-fifth) on a jahili treasure is agreed upon. The dispute is only about the remaining four-fifths. The passage also distinguishes between owned and unowned land.
The term 'jahili' refers to pre-Islamic artifacts with pagan symbols or names of non-Muslim kings.
2
Identify the Two Positions
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the four-fifths go to the mukhtatt lahu (the person to whom the land was allotted). Abu Yusuf: they go to the finder (al-wajid).
The mukhtatt lahu is the one who received the land from the Imam during the division of conquered lands.
3
Analyze the Proof of the Majority
Their proof is that the hand of the mukhtatt lahu preceded—he owns the land and therefore owns what is in its interior. This is analogous to a fish with a pearl: if a man catches a fish with a pearl inside and sells the fish, the pearl does not transfer to the buyer unless specified, because it is considered deposited (mawdi').
This analogy shows that treasure is not part of the land like a mine; it remains owned by the landowner unless explicitly transferred.
4
Analyze Abu Yusuf's Proof
Abu Yusuf argues that entitlement is through complete possession (tamam al-hiyaza), and the finder is the one who physically possesses the treasure. He analogizes it to a mine (ma'din), where the finder gets the remainder after khums.
For Abu Yusuf, physical possession is the decisive factor, not prior ownership of the land.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence
If the finder is different from the allottee, under the majority view the finder gets nothing; under Abu Yusuf he gets the four-fifths. The mufta bihi is the majority view, so the allottee prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الزكاة باب الركاز ٤١١ لأنه وفي التحفة: جعله لبيت المال إن لم يعرف الأقصى وورثته وهذا كله عندهما. وقال أبو يوسف : إن الباقي للواجد كالمعدن لأن الاستحقاق بتمام الحيازة وهي منه. ولهما: إن يد المختط له سبقت إليه وهي يد الخصوص فيملك به ما في الباطن وإن كانت على الظاهر كما إذا اصطاد سمكة في بطنها درة ثم بالبيع لم تخرج عن ملكه لأنه مودع فيها بخلاف المعدن من أجزائها فينتقل إلى المشتري ومحل الخلاف فيما إذا لم يدعه مالك الأرض، فإن ادعى أنه ملكه فالقول قوله اتفاقاً. كذا في المعراج أطلق في الكنز فشمل النقد وغيره من السلاح والآلات وأثاث المنازل والفصوص والقماش لأنها كانت ملكاً للكفارة فحوته أيدينا قهراً فصارت غنيمة وقيدناه بدفين الجاهلية بأن كان نقشه صنماً أو اسم ملوكهم المعروفين للاحتراز عن دفين أهل الإسلام كالمكتوب عليه كلمة الشهادة أو نقش آخر معروف للمسلمين فهو لقطة لأن مال المسلمين لا يغنم وحكمها معروف، وإن اشتبه الضرب عليهم فهو جاهلي في ظاهر المذهب لأنه الأصل، وقيل يجعل إسلامياً في زماننا لتقادم العهد. وأشار بقوله «للمختط له» إلى وجده في أرض مملوكه لأنه لو وجده في أرض غير مملوكة كالجبال والمفازة فهو كالمعدن يجب خمسه وباقيه للواجد، مطلقاً حراً كان أو عبداً كما ذكرناه. وفي المغرب: الخطة المكان المختط لبناء دار أو غير ذلك في العمارات. وفي المعراج : إنما قالوا للمختط له لأن الإمام إذا أراد قسمه الأراضي يخط لكل واحد من الغانمين ويجعل تلك الناحية له . أنه
Book of Zakat, Chapter of Rikaz, page 411. 'Because' And in al-Tuhfah: He made it for the public treasury if the farthest [owner] and his heirs are not known, and all this according to them both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And Abu Yusuf said: The remainder is for the finder like the mine, because entitlement is through complete possession, which is from him. And for them both (the Sahibayn): The hand of the mukhtatt lahu preceded to it, and it is a hand of special ownership, so he owns by it what is in the interior even though it is on the exterior, just as if he catches a fish with a pearl in its belly and then sells it, the pearl does not leave his ownership because it is deposited in it, unlike the mine which is part of its parts and thus transfers to the buyer. And the place of dispute is when the owner of the land does not claim it; if he claims it is his, his word is accepted by agreement. This is in al-Mi'raj. He made absolute in the treasure, so it includes cash and other things: weapons, tools, furniture, gems, and fabrics because they were the property of the disbelievers and our hands seized them by force, so they became ghanimah. And we restricted it to jahili burial such that its engraving is an idol or the names of their known kings, to exclude burial of the people of Islam, such as that upon which the testimony of faith is written or another known Islamic inscription; that is luqatah because the property of Muslims is not ghanimah, and its ruling is known. And if the type (style) is ambiguous, it is jahili in the apparent madhhab because it is the original; and it is said: it is made Islamic in our time due to the long passage of time. And he indicated by his statement 'for the mukhtatt lahu' that it is found in owned land, because if he finds it in unowned land like mountains and deserts, it is like the mine: khums is wajib and the remainder is for the finder, absolutely whether free or slave as we mentioned. And in al-Mughrib: al-khittah is the place marked out for building a house or other things in settled areas. And in al-Mi'raj: They only said 'for the mukhtatt lahu' because when the Imam wants to divide the lands, he marks out for each of the ghanimun and makes that area for him.

More likely, 'ولهما' refers to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) as the majority against Abu Yusuf. So 'ولهما' likely refers to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The passage then explains their proof: the hand of the mukhtatt lahu preceded (i.e., his ownership of the land includes what is beneath it), analogous to a fish with a pearl: the seller of the fish does not sell the pearl unless specified. But the text says 'بخلاف المعدن من أجزائها فينتقل إلى المشتري' meaning the mine is part of the land so it transfers, but treasure is not. The passage then specifies that this dispute is when the owner of the land does not claim the treasure; if he claims it, his word is accepted. The remainder discusses the definition of kanz: it must be jahili (pre-Islamic) with pagan symbols or names of their kings; if Islamic, it is luqatah (lost property). If unclear, it is presumed jahili in the apparent madhhab, but in later times it may be considered Islamic due to antiquity. Finally, it reiterates that the ruling 'for the mukhtatt lahu' applies when the treasure is found in owned land; if found in unowned land (mountains, desert), it is like a mine: khums is wajib and the remainder goes to the finder.

Key Terms
المختط له (al-mukhtatt lahu): The person to whom the Imam allots a plot of land for building or cultivation.
حيازة (hiyaza): Physical possession, a basis for ownership in some rulings.
كنز (kanz): A buried treasure, specifically from the pre-Islamic (jahili) period.
غالب الذهب (jaliyy): Clear, apparent; here used for the classification of the treasure's origin.
