Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a detailed commentary on the chapter of 'Ushr from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It covers several key rulings. The first major discussion is the nisab for 'ushr. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that no nisab is required and it need not remain throughout the season, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require a nisab of 5 awsuq and its continuance. The author provides proofs: the Sahibayn cite the hadith 'No sadaqah on grain or dates until it reaches 5 awsuq'; Abu Hanifa relies on the general Quranic command and the hadith 'In what is watered by the sky, a tenth', interpreting the Sahibayn's hadith as referring to zakat of trade. Next, the text discusses what is exempt from 'ushr: items not intended for land exploitation like firewood, reeds, straw, and seeds not for cultivation; also things attached to land like bees and trees. For produce not measured by wasq (e.g., saffron, cotton), Abu Yusuf equates nisab to value of the least wasq-measured crop (e.g., maize), while Muhammad sets a fixed weight (e.g., 5 loads of cotton, 5 manns of saffron). For sugarcane, Abu Yusuf uses value of 5 awsuq of the least crop, Muhammad says 5 manns of sugar. Then the ruling on irrigation: rain-fed land requires full 'ushr (10%), artificially irrigated land requires half (5%). If mixed, the majority method applies; if equal, half 'ushr is given. Expenses are never deducted because the Prophet (ﷺ) set the rates based on typical costs. Finally, the passage covers land tax categories: when a taghlabi (non-Muslim subject) owns 'ushri land, 'ushr is doubled; this double remains even if he converts or sells to a Muslim. If a dhimmi buys 'ushri land from a Muslim, it becomes kharaj land. Abu Yusuf disagrees on some points, but the relied-upon positions are given.

Key Terms
غرب (gharb): A large bucket used for drawing water from a well or river, typically used for irrigation.
دالية (dāliyah): A large waterwheel operated by animals, used for irrigation.
تضعيف (taḍʿīf): Doubling of 'ushr on land bought by a taghlabi (Christian Arab).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject) buys 'ushri land from a Muslim, what happens to the land's obligation?
A) It remains 'ushri
B) It becomes kharaji (land tax)
C) 'Ushr is doubled
D) No tax is due

Key Takeaway
The rate of 'ushr depends on irrigation method; expenses are not deductible; land category changes upon sale to non-Muslims (taghlabi: double; dhimmi: kharaj).

The 'Ushr on Sugar Cane and Its Custom-Based Nisab
Ruling statement with 'urf-based nisab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligation of 'ushr on sugar cane and explain how its nisab is determined by custom ('urf) rather than a fixed textual measure.

Core Idea
Sugar cane requires 'ushr, and its nisab is set by custom—five tons in this case.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the zakat on sugar cane. Unlike staple crops whose nisab is fixed by hadith, sugar cane's nisab is determined by local custom. The commentator applies the principle that 'urf can define thresholds for non-staple agricultural produce, leading to a practical weight-based nisab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the crop and the obligation
The text states that sugar (i.e., sugar cane) is subject to 'ushr. Recognize that 'ushr is a form of zakat on agricultural produce.
The verb 'وجب' indicates obligation.
2
Note that nisab is not fixed by text but by custom
The commentator says 'as in the custom of our lands' (كما في عرف ديارنا). Understand that the Hanafi school allows 'urf to set the threshold for non-staple crops.
This contrasts with staple crops like wheat and barley, which have a fixed nisab of 5 wasq.
3
Understand the Hanafi principle of 'urf
The Hanafi school uses reliable custom to determine legal thresholds when no explicit textual evidence exists, provided the custom is widespread and consistent.
This principle is rooted in the maxim: 'Custom is a determining factor' (العرف محكّم).
4
Apply the nisab given
The text specifies five tons as the nisab in the commentator's region. Recognize that this number is region-specific; in other regions, a different custom may apply.
The weight is in modern tons; in the original context, it may have referred to a regional unit.
5
Compare with other schools
Contrast the Hanafi position with the Shafi'i school, which requires a nisab of 5 wasq (approx. 653 kg) for all crops, including sugar cane.
This difference has practical implications for calculating zakat on non-staple produce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سكر وجب فيه العشر على قوله وينبغي أن يكون نصاب القصب عنده خمسة أطنان كما في عرف ديارنا .
Sugar: 'ushr is obligatory upon it according to his statement. And it should be that the nisab of sugar cane according to him is five tons, as in the custom of our lands.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the commentary on the matn. The matn likely mentioned 'sugar' as a crop subject to 'ushr. The commentator notes that according to one view (possibly that of the author), the nisab for sugar cane is five tons, based on the prevailing custom ('urf) in their region. This reflects the Hanafi allowance of 'urf to determine nisab for non-staple crops. The phrase 'على قوله' may indicate a difference of opinion, perhaps referring to another source.

Key Terms
العشر (al-'ushr): The tithe (one-tenth) due on agricultural produce from land irrigated naturally.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum quantity of produce that triggers the obligation of 'ushr.
قصب (qasab): Sugar cane, a crop from which sugar is extracted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Sugar cane is subject to 'ushr, and its nisab is determined by custom—here five tons—demonstrating the Hanafi use of 'urf for non-staple crops.

Half 'Ushr for Machine-Irrigated Land
Expansion of the matn on half 'ushr for machine irrigation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakat for machine-irrigated land, apply the majority rule for mixed irrigation, and evaluate the difference with Al-Ghayah's view on equal irrigation.

Core Idea
Machine-irrigated land pays half 'ushr; equal mixed irrigation does not raise it to full 'ushr.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the Hanafi ruling on zakat for land irrigated by artificial means. It defines two irrigation devices, sets the half 'ushr rate, and provides a method for mixed irrigation. Understanding this distinction is key for proper zakat calculation on agricultural produce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognise that the text specifies half 'ushr (half the standard rate) for land irrigated by a bucket (gharb) or waterwheel (daliyah). The ruling is wajib.
The standard 'ushr applies to rain-irrigated land.
2
Locate the dalil
The commentator cites 'for the hadith' (للحديث) without quoting; this refers to a known hadith distinguishing between natural and artificial irrigation.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut but accepted in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Apply the majority rule for mixed irrigation
If part of the year is irrigated by machine and part by other means (e.g., rain), the method used for the majority of the growing season determines the rate.
This is analogous to the rule for grazing versus fed cattle.
4
Consider the equal case
If irrigation methods are equal in duration, half 'ushr is obligatory. The reason given is 'consideration for the poor' (نظراً للفقراء).
This differs from Al-Ghayah, which suggests three-quarters 'ushr.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
The text mentions that the apparent meaning of Al-Ghayah is three-quarters 'ushr when equal. The author does not resolve this, implying the half 'ushr view is relied upon.
The majority of later Hanafi works adopt half 'ushr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ونصفه في مسقى غرب ودالية أي ويجب نصف العشر فيما سقي بآلة للحديث والغرب دلو عظيم والدالية دولاب عظيم تدبره البقر. وإن سقي بعض السنة بآلة والبعض بغيرها فالمعتبر أكثرها كما مر في السائمة والعلوفة وإن استويا يجب نصف العشر نظراً للفقراء كما في السائمة، وظاهر الغاية وجوب ثلاثة أرباع العشر
His statement: 'and half of it in irrigated (land) with bucket and waterwheel' – meaning half of the 'ushr is obligatory on what is irrigated by machine, due to the hadith. Al-gharb is a large bucket, and al-daliyah is a large wheel operated by cattle. If part of the year it is irrigated by machine and part by other means, the majority is considered, as passed in the case of grazing cattle and fodder. If they are equal, half 'ushr is obligatory, looking to the benefit of the poor, as in the grazing case. The apparent (meaning) of Al-Ghayah is the obligation of three-quarters of the 'ushr.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This is an expansion probe of the matn. The commentator explains the matn phrase 'and half of it' referring to half 'ushr for land irrigated by artificial means like a bucket (gharb) or waterwheel (daliyah). The proof is a hadith (not quoted). For land that is partly irrigated by machine and partly by rain, the majority determines the rate; if equal, half 'ushr is given. The commentator mentions a difference with the author of Al-Ghayah who suggests three-quarters 'ushr when equal. This shows the Hanafi use of qiyas to similar cases (grazing animals).

Key Terms
الغرب (al-gharb): A large bucket used for drawing water from a well.
الدالية (al-daliyah): A large waterwheel operated by cattle.
مسقى (masqa): Irrigated land, with reference to the method of irrigation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Full 'ushr
B) Half 'ushr
C) Three-quarters 'ushr
D) No 'ushr

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school sets the rate for machine-irrigated land at half 'ushr (5%), and for mixed irrigation, the dominant method determines the rate, with equality favouring half 'ushr.

Expenses Not Deducted for 'Ushr Calculation
Expands the matn phrase 'expenses are not deducted' with examples and evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that production expenses are not deducted before calculating 'ushr and explain the Prophetic evidence.

Core Idea
Expenses of cultivation are not deducted; 'ushr is on total produce because the rate already accounts for difficulty.

This lesson examines a crucial rule in agricultural zakat: the total produce is the base for 'ushr, not the profit after expenses. We will see the Prophetic reasoning and correct a common misconception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The matn says 'expenses are not deducted'. Understand that this is a clear prohibition of deducting any costs before paying 'ushr.
2
List Example Expenses
The commentator lists wages, cattle feed, canal digging, and guard pay. These are illustrative; any cost of production is included.
3
Understand the Evidence
The Prophet ruled that the obligation varies with the expense. This means the rate (full or half) already accounts for the effort, so further deduction would be double-counting.
4
Apply the Rule
The ruling applies to all produce subject to 'ushr, whether full or half. The obligation is on the entire harvest, not the net profit.
Correct the misconception that one can deduct expenses first and pay on the remainder.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا ترفع المؤن) أي لا تحسب أجرة العمال ونفقة البقر وكري الأنهار وأجرة الحافظ وغير ذلك لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حكم بتفاوت الواجب لتفاوت المؤنة فلا معنى لرفعها. أطلقه فشمل ما فيه العشر وما فيه نصفه فيجب إخراج الواجب من جميع ما أخرجته الأرض عشراً أو نصفاً إلا أن ما تكلفه يأخذه بلا عشر أو نصفه ثم يخرج الواجب من الباقي كما توهمه بعض الناس
His statement: 'and expenses are not deducted' – meaning the wages of workers, expenditure on cattle, digging of canals, guard's pay, and other things are not calculated, because the Prophet ﷺ ruled the variation of the obligation according to the variation of expense, so there is no meaning to their deduction. It is general and includes that which has 'ushr and that which has half of it; so the obligation must be taken from all that the land produces, whether 'ushr or half, except that some people mistakenly think that he takes what he spent without 'ushr or half and then gives the obligation from the remainder.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This is an expansion probe of the matn phrase 'expenses are not deducted'. The commentator clarifies that costs of production (wages, animal feed, canal digging, guard) are not subtracted from the total produce before calculating 'ushr. The rationale is from a hadith of the Prophet ﷺ that the obligation varies with the expense, meaning that the rate (full or half) already accounts for difficulty, so further deduction is unwarranted. The commentator emphasizes the generality: whether the produce is subject to full or half 'ushr, the obligation applies to the entire output. He corrects a misunderstanding some people have that they can first take their costs tax-free and then pay 'ushr on the remainder.

Key Terms
المؤن (al-mu'an): Expenses and costs incurred in agricultural production.
رفع المؤن (raf' al-mu'an): Deducting or subtracting expenses from the produce for 'ushr calculation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, are expenses like workers' wages deducted before paying 'ushr?
A) Yes, they are deducted
B) No, they are not deducted
C) Only irrigation costs are deducted
D) Only if the farmer is poor

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, production expenses are not deducted from agricultural produce before calculating 'ushr; the obligation is on the total output.

Doubled 'Ushr for Taghlabi Land Purchases: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on persistence of doubled 'ushr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on doubled 'ushr for Taghlabi land purchases and explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding its persistence.

Core Idea
Doubled 'ushr on Taghlabi-purchased land persists even after conversion or sale, like kharaj.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks a distinct Hanafi rule: when a Taghlabi (a Christian from Banu Taghlib) buys 'ushri land from a Muslim, he pays double the normal 'ushr. The commentator then probes whether this doubling continues if the Taghlabi converts to Islam or sells the land to a Muslim or dhimmi. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad maintain it does, while Abu Yusuf argues it should revert to a single 'ushr. Understanding this dispute clarifies how land tax obligations can become attached to the land itself, independent of the owner's status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the passage and list the three cases discussed: (1) Taghlabi buys 'ushri land, (2) Taghlabi converts to Islam, (3) land sold to a Muslim or dhimmi.
The text explicitly numbers them as 'three issues'.
2
State the ruling in each scenario
For each scenario, determine whether the doubled 'ushr applies or not according to the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).
In all three, the ruling is doubled 'ushr.
3
Identify the basis for the ruling
The first scenario rests on consensus of the Companions (ijma). The second and third rely on analogy (qiyas) to kharaj, which is a permanent land tax regardless of owner.
Analogy: the land acquires the function (wazifa) of doubled 'ushr, just as kharaj land keeps its kharaj.
4
Note Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf argues that the reason for doubling (the Taghlabi's non-Muslim status) disappears upon conversion or sale to a Muslim, so the extra 'ushr should cease.
He reduces it to a single 'ushr in the latter two scenarios.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the doubled 'ushr continues; under Abu Yusuf, it becomes single. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's.
The text implies the majority view is the madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وضعفه في أرض عشرية لتغلبي وإن أسلم أو ابتاعها منه مسلم أو ذمي) أي يجب عشران في أرض إلى آخره وفيه ثلاث مسائل : الأولى الأرض العشرية إذا اشتراها تغلبي فالمذهب تضعيفه عليه لإجماع الصحابة الثانية إذا أسلم التغلبي فالتضعيف باق عليه لأن التضعيف صار وظيفة الأرض فيبقى بعد إسلامه كالخراج الثالثة إذا اشتراها منه مسلم أو فكذلك لأنها انتقلت إليه بوظيفتها كالخراج فإن المسلم أهل للبقاء عليه وإن لم يكن أهلاً لابتدائه، ورد الواجب أبو يوسف في المسألتين إلى عشر واحد لزوال الداعي إلى التضعيف
His statement: 'and double it on 'ushri land for a Taghlabi, and if he becomes Muslim or a Muslim or a dhimmi buys it from him' – that is, two 'ushrs are obligatory on the land, etc. It contains three issues: First: if a Taghlabi buys 'ushri land, the school (madhhab) is to double it upon him due to the consensus of the Companions. Second: if the Taghlabi becomes Muslim, the doubling remains upon him because the doubling became a function of the land, so it remains after his Islam like kharaj. Third: if a Muslim buys it from him, similarly, because it transfers to him with its function like kharaj; the Muslim is capable of its continuance even if not capable for its inception. Abu Yusuf reduced the obligation in the two issues to a single 'ushr due to the removal of the reason for doubling.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This is an expansion probe of the matn discussing a distinctive Hanafi rule. The matn states that 'ushr is doubled on land bought by a Taghlabi (a Christian from the Banu Taghlib tribe) from a Muslim. The commentator explains three scenarios from the school: (1) The Taghlabi buyer pays doubled 'ushr by consensus of the Companions. (2) If the Taghlabi later converts to Islam, the doubled 'ushr remains as a burden on the land (like kharaj). (3) If a Muslim or dhimmi buys the land from the Taghlabi, the doubled 'ushr continues because the land's function transfers. Abu Yusuf disagrees: he says the doubling ceases when the reason (the buyer being Taghlabi) is removed; only a single 'ushr is due. Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
التغلبي (al-taghlabi): A Christian from the Banu Taghlib tribe, treated differently from other dhimmis in Hanafi tax law.
تضعيف العشر (tadh'if al-'ushr): The imposition of double the normal 'ushr rate (two 'ushrs) on certain land.
وظيفة الأرض (wazifat al-ard): The permanent tax burden attached to the land itself, like kharaj or doubled 'ushr, independent of the owner's status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the basis for the rule that a Taghlabi who buys 'ushri land must pay doubled 'ushr?
A) The Quranic verse on zakat
B) Consensus of the Companions
C) Qiyas on kharaj land
D) The opinion of Abu Yusuf

Key Takeaway
The doubled 'ushr on land purchased by a Taghlabi becomes a permanent function of the land, persisting even after conversion or sale, based on qiyas to kharaj.

Kharaj Due from Dhimmi Buying 'Ushri Land
Ruling with proof (talil) on land tax shift for dhimmi buyer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a dhimmi buys 'ushri land from a Muslim and explain the legal reasoning (the illa) that distinguishes kharaj from 'ushr.

Core Idea
Kharaj is a penalty applicable to any owner; 'ushr is a devotional act impossible for a non-Muslim.

The passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses a specific land tax scenario: when a dhimmi (non-Taghlabi non-Muslim) purchases land that was previously 'ushri (subject to the tithe) from a Muslim. The Hanafi school rules that the dhimmi must pay kharaj instead of 'ushr. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind this ruling and the rebuttals to opposing Hanafi views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a dhimmi buying 'ushri land from a Muslim. The ruling as stated: 'kharaj is obligatory' (yajib al-kharaj).
The ruling is wajib, not fard, because it is a financial obligation.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the reasoning (talil) provided: 'ushr is a devotional meaning (ma'na al-ibada), which is incompatible with disbelief (kufr). Kharaj is a penalty (uqubah) and Islam does not oppose it.
The proof is not a direct text from Quran or hadith but a rational deduction based on the nature of the taxes.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause (illa) for not applying 'ushr is that it is an act of worship requiring faith (iman). Since the dhimmi lacks faith, the obligation of 'ushr cannot attach. Kharaj, being a worldly penalty, does not require faith.
This is a clear application of the principle that devotional acts are contingent upon capacity (ahliyyah al-ada).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf held that the 'ushr should be doubled; Muhammad held that 'ushr remains. The text rebuts both, affirming kharaj.
The rebuttal argues that doubling 'ushr is baseless because the reason for exemption (kufr) applies equally, and retaining 'ushr ignores the devotional incompatibility.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The position that kharaj is due from the dhimmi buyer is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) opinion, as supported by the reasoning.
This is consistent with the principle that penalties are not faith-dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وخراج إن اشترى ذمي أرضاً عشرية من مسلم) أي يجب الخراج لأن العشر معنى العبادة والكفر ينافيها ولا وجه إلى التضعيف لأن الكلام في غير التغلبي بخلاف الخراج لأنه عقوبة والإسلام لا ينافيها كالرق، وبه اندفع قول أبي يوسف من تضعيف العشر عليه وقول محمد ببقاء العشر
His statement: 'and kharaj if a dhimmi buys 'ushri land from a Muslim' – that is, kharaj is obligatory, because 'ushr is a devotional meaning and disbelief opposes it, and there is no basis for doubling because the discussion is about someone other than a Taghlabi, unlike kharaj because it is a penalty and Islam does not oppose it, like slavery. And by this is rebutted the statement of Abu Yusuf of doubling the 'ushr upon him and the statement of Muhammad of retaining the 'ushr.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof (talil). The matn says that if a dhimmi (non-Taghlabi) buys 'ushri land from a Muslim, kharaj (land tax) becomes due, not 'ushr. The commentator explains the reasoning: 'ushr is an act of worship (عبادة) which is incompatible with disbelief (kufr). Since the dhimmi is not a Muslim, he cannot perform that devotional obligation. In contrast, kharaj is a penalty (عقوبة) imposed on the land regardless of the owner's faith, like slavery which also applies to Muslims. The commentator notes that this rebuts Abu Yusuf's view that doubled 'ushr should apply, and Muhammad's view that 'ushr remains. So the agreed Hanafi position is that kharaj becomes due.

Key Terms
الخراج (al-kharaj): A land tax on land that produces revenue, often contrasted with 'ushr.
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under Muslim rule with a covenant of protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a dhimmi who buys 'ushri land not pay 'ushr?
A) Because the land becomes kharaji automatically.
B) Because 'ushr is an act of worship and kufr is incompatible with it.
C) Because the dhimmi is exempt from all taxes.
D) Because the sale transfers the tax liability to the seller.

Key Takeaway
When a dhimmi buys 'ushri land, kharaj replaces 'ushr because the latter is devotional and requires faith, while kharaj is a penalty applicable to all.

Summary of Land Tax Cases by Buyer Type and Land Type
Summarizes land tax rules per buyer type and land type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the tax classification outcome when a Muslim, dhimmi, or Taghlabi purchases 'ushri, kharaji, or tadh'ifi land, and identify the points of disagreement among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Land tax categories usually persist: only a dhimmi buying 'ushri land (making it kharaji) and a Taghlabi buying 'ushri land (doubled 'ushr) change the status.

Determining the tax status of land after a sale is a complex area of Hanafi fiqh, involving three land types and three buyer categories. This summary passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq concisely maps out the rulings, including the points of divergence among the founding imams of the school. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying land tax laws in historical and contemporary contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three land types and three buyer categories
The passage lists land as 'ushri, kharaji, or tadh'ifi. Buyers are Muslim, dhimmi (non-Taghlabi), or Taghlabi. Make a mental table with these three rows and three columns.
2
Trace the ruling for a Muslim buyer across all three land types
When a Muslim buys 'ushri or kharaji land, it remains as is. When buying tadh'ifi land, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad it remains tadh'ifi, but Abu Yusuf holds it returns to single 'ushr.
Note the first intra-Hanafi disagreement.
3
Trace the ruling for a Taghlabi buyer
A Taghlabi buying kharaji or tadh'ifi land: it remains kharaji/tadh'ifi. Buying 'ushri from a Muslim: according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the 'ushr is doubled (making it tadh'ifi), contrary to Muhammad.
Second disagreement: Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad.
4
Trace the ruling for a dhimmi (non-Taghlabi) buyer
A dhimmi buying kharaji or tadh'ifi: remains as is. Buying 'ushri: it becomes kharaji once established in ownership. Note that Al-Mukhtasar did not stipulate possession (qabd) for kharaj to be obligatory, while Al-Hidayah did.
The only case where land changes category from 'ushri to kharaji.
5
Summarize the pattern and exceptions
Default: land tax status remains unchanged. Two exceptions: (1) dhimmi buys 'ushri → becomes kharaji; (2) Taghlabi buys 'ushri → becomes tadh'ifi (doubled 'ushr). Internal disagreements affect only the tadh'ifi cases for Muslim and Taghlabi buyers.
Memorize the exceptions and the differing imams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل هذه المسائل أن الأرض إما عشرية أو خراجية أو تضعيفية، والمشترون مسلم وذمي وتغلبي فالمسلم إذا اشترى العشرية أو الخراجية بقيت على حالها أو التضعيفية فكذلك عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد. وقال أبو يوسف: ترجع إلى عشر واحد فإذا اشترى التغلبي الخراجية بقيت خراجية أو التضعيفية فهي تضعيفية أو العشرية من مسلم ضوعف عليه العشر عندهما خلافاً لمحمد، وإذا اشترى ذمي غير تغلبي خراجية أو تضعيفية بقيت على حالها أو عشرية صارت خراجية إن استقرت في ملكه عنده، ولم يشترط القبض في المختصر لوجوب الخراج وشرطه في الهداية لأن ذمي
And the conclusion of these issues is that land is either 'ushri, kharaji, or tadh'ifi. The buyers are Muslim, dhimmi, and Taghlabi. So a Muslim, if he buys 'ushri or kharaji land, it remains as it was; if he buys tadh'ifi land, similarly according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf says: it returns to a single 'ushr. When a Taghlabi buys kharaji land, it remains kharaji; if he buys tadh'ifi, it is tadh'ifi; if he buys 'ushri from a Muslim, it is doubled upon him according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), contrary to Muhammad. And if a dhimmi other than Taghlabi buys kharaji or tadh'ifi land, it remains as it was; if he buys 'ushri land, it becomes kharaji provided it is established in his ownership according to him. And he did not stipulate possession in Al-Mukhtasar for the obligation of kharaj, but he stipulated it in Al-Hidayah because a dhimmi...
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This is a summary paragraph that systematically tabulates the tax classification of land based on the type of buyer and the original land type. The commentator presents the views of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (usually agreeing) and the dissenting view of Abu Yusuf. The summary covers: (1) Muslim buyer keeps land as is. (2) Taghlabi buyer: kharaji land stays kharaji; tadh'ifi stays tadh'ifi; 'ushri land becomes doubled. (3) Dhimmi (non-Taghlabi): kharaji/tadh'ifi unchanged; 'ushri land becomes kharaji. There is also a note on when possession is required for kharaj, differing between Al-Mukhtasar and Al-Hidayah. This passage serves as a useful mnemonic for the complex rules.

Key Terms
تضعيفية (tadh'ifiyyah): Land that is subject to doubled 'ushr (two 'ushrs) due to previous acquisition by a Taghlabi.
خراجية (kharajiyyah): Land subject to kharaj tax.
عشرية ('ushriyyah): Land subject to 'ushr tax (one-tenth of produce).
تغلبي (Taghlabi): A member of the Banu Taghlib tribe, treated as dhimmi but sometimes given different land tax rules.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens when a dhimmi (non-Taghlabi) buys 'ushri land from a Muslim?
A) It remains 'ushri
B) It becomes kharaji
C) It becomes tadh'ifi
D) It becomes tax-free

Key Takeaway
Land tax status generally persists after transfer, with two key exceptions: a dhimmi buying 'ushri makes it kharaji, and a Taghlabi buying 'ushri makes it tadh'ifi (doubled 'ushr), subject to intra-Hanafi disagreements.

Pre-emption and Rescission: How Land Tax Status Changes
Explains tax status change upon pre-emption and rescission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for how pre-emption (shuf'ah) and judicial rescission (faskh) affect whether land is 'ushri or kharaji, and distinguish the ruling for iqalah.

Core Idea
Pre-emption and judicial rescission revert land to 'ushri; iqalah keeps it kharaji.

The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a subtle point: when land moves between a Muslim and a dhimmi, its tax classification (kharaji vs 'ushri) depends on the mechanism of transfer. Pre-emption by a Muslim and judicial rescission treat the sale as void ab initio, restoring the original tax status. However, mutual cancellation (iqalah) is treated as a new sale, preserving the kharaji status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two cases: (1) a Muslim takes land from a dhimmi by pre-emption, and (2) a dhimmi returns land to a Muslim seller via rescission (faskh). In both, the land becomes 'ushri.
2
Understand the pre-emption principle
Pre-emption shifts the transaction to the pre-emptor, as if he bought directly from the original Muslim seller. Therefore, the land retains its original 'ushri status.
The pre-emptor is treated as the original buyer from the Muslim.
3
Analyze judicial rescission
When the dhimmi returns the land due to a defect or option, and the return is by judicial decree (faskh bi-qada), the sale is annulled as if it never occurred. Hence, the Muslim seller regains the land as 'ushri.
The seller's right to return was not extinguished by the defective sale.
4
Distinguish iqalah
If the return is by mutual agreement (iqalah) or out-of-court, it is considered a new sale from the dhimmi to the Muslim. The land remains kharaji because the dhimmi's ownership was valid and the new sale transfers its tax status.
Iqalah is a rescission only between the parties, but a new sale for third parties (e.g., tax authorities).
5
Apply the rule
Recognize that the presence of kharaj on the land is not a new defect preventing the dhimmi's return, because it can be removed by judicial rescission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعشران أخذها مسلم بالشفعة أورد على البائع للفساد أما الأول فلتحول الصفقة إلى الشفيع كأنه اشتراها من المسلم، وأما الثاني فلأنه بالرد والفسخ جعل البيع كأنه لم يكن لأن حق المسلم وهو البائع لم ينقطع بهذا البيع لكونه مستحق الرد. وأشار بقوله للفساد» إلى كل موضع كان الرد فيه فسخاً كالرد بخيار الشرط والرؤية مطلقاً والرد بخيار العيب إن كان بقضاء، وأما بغير قضاء فهي خراجية على حالها كالإقالة لأنها فسخ في حق المتعاقدين بيع جديد في حق ثالث فصار شراء من الذمي فتنتقل إلى المسلم بوظيفتها فاستفيد من وضع المسألة أن للذمي أن يردها بعيب قديم ولا يكون وجوب الخراج عليها عيباً حادثاً لأنه يرتفع بالفسخ بالقضاء فلا يمنع الرد
And two 'ushrs: if a Muslim takes it by pre-emption, he returns (the land) to the seller due to invalidity. As for the first, because the transaction shifts to the pre-emptor as if he bought it from the Muslim. As for the second, because by the return and rescission, the sale is made as if it never occurred, because the right of the Muslim (the seller) was not cut off by this sale due to it being subject to return. And he indicated by his saying 'due to invalidity' every case where the return is a rescission, like return by option of stipulation and inspection absolutely, and return by option of defect if it is by judicial decree. But if without judicial decree, it remains kharaji as it is, like iqalah, because it is a rescission between the two contracting parties but a new sale regarding a third party, so it becomes a purchase from the dhimmi and transfers to the Muslim with its function. From the framing of the issue it is understood that the dhimmi may return it due to an old defect, and the obligation of kharaj upon it is not a new defect, because it is removed by judicial rescission, so it does not prevent the return.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This is a ruling with talil. The passage discusses two scenarios: (1) If a Muslim exercises pre-emption (shuf'a) to take land that a dhimmi had bought from a Muslim, the land becomes 'ushri (the original status) because the pre-emptor is seen as buying from the original Muslim seller. (2) If the dhimmi returns the land to the original Muslim seller due to a defect or option, and the return is a proper rescission (faskh) by judicial decree, the land also reverts to 'ushri. However, if the return is by mutual cancellation (iqalah) or out-of-court, it is considered a new sale from the dhimmi to the Muslim, and the land retains its kharaji status. The passage also notes that the presence of kharaj on the land is not a new defect that would allow the dhimmi to return it, because it can be removed by judicial rescission.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption, allowing a co-owner or neighbor to purchase property that was sold to an outsider.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract, often by judicial decree.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a Muslim takes land from a dhimmi by pre-emption, what is the tax status of the land?
A) It remains kharaji
B) It becomes 'ushri
C) It becomes tadh'ifi
D) It becomes tax-free

Key Takeaway
Pre-emption and judicial rescission revert land to 'ushri; iqalah keeps it kharaji because it is a new sale for tax purposes.

Muslim's Garden Irrigated with Kharaj Water
Explains tax determination by water source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on how water source determines whether a Muslim's garden is subject to 'ushr or kharaj, and explain the resolution of the intra-Hanafi dispute on voluntary use of kharaj water.

Core Idea
The water source determines the tax; kharaj is valid if voluntarily chosen.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical scenario: a Muslim converts his residential house into a garden. The tax obligation—whether 'ushr or kharaj—hinges on the water used for irrigation. The text presents the base rule, then engages with an objection raised by al-'Utabi and the reconciliatory response of the commentator. Understanding this clarifies the boundaries between compulsory and voluntary tax liability in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scenarios
The text states that if a Muslim makes his house a garden, the tax depends on the water. There are three scenarios: irrigation solely with 'ushr water → 'ushri; solely with kharaj water → kharaji; mixed equally → 'ushr prevails.
The ruling is from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq.
2
Understand the objection by al-'Utabi
Al-'Utabi questioned how kharaj could be obligatory on a Muslim initially, since kharaj is traditionally associated with non-Muslims or conquered land.
The source of objection is the apparent injustice of imposing kharaj on a Muslim.
3
Examine the reconciling answer
The commentator responds that compulsory imposition of kharaj is prohibited, but if the Muslim voluntarily chooses to use kharaj water, it becomes permissible. This is analogous to reviving dead land with the imam's permission and using kharaj water.
The key distinction: choice vs. compulsion.
4
Note the restriction to 'making it a garden'
The ruling is limited to cases where the house is converted into a garden (with a wall and scattered trees). If the land has date palms without being made a garden, no tax is due (they are like natural growth).
This restriction avoids automatic tax liability on pre-existing trees.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن جعل مسلم داره بستاناً فمؤنته تدور مائه) مع يعني فإن سقاه بماء العشر فهو عشري، وإن سقاه بماء الخراج فهو خراجي، وإن سقاه مرة من ماء العشر ومرة من ماء الخراج فعليه العشر لأنه أحق بالعشر من الخراج. كذا في غاية البيان. واستشكل العتابي وجوب الخراج على المسلم ابتداء حتى نقل في غاية البيان أن الإمام السرخسي ذكر في كتاب الجامع أن عليه العشر بكل حال لأنه أحق بالعشر من الخراج وهو الأظهر ا هـ. وجوابه أن الممنوع وضع الخراج عليه ابتداء جبراً، أما باختياره فيجوز وقد اختاره هنا حيث سقاه بماء الخراج فهو كما إذا أحيا أرضاً ميتة بإذن الإمام وسقاها بماء الخراج فإنه يجب عليه الخراج والبستان يحوط عليها حائط وفيها أشجار متفرقة. كذا في المعراج. قيد بجعلها بستاناً لأنه لو لم يجعلها بستاناً وفيها نخل تغل أكراراً لا شيء فيها
His statement: 'and if a Muslim makes his house into a garden, then its expense depends on its water'. That is, if he irrigates it with 'ushr water, it is 'ushri; if he irrigates it with kharaj water, it is kharaji; and if he irrigates it once with 'ushr water and once with kharaj water, then upon him is 'ushr because it is more entitled to 'ushr than to kharaj. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. But al-'Utabi found problematic the obligation of kharaj on a Muslim initially, until it was mentioned in Ghayat al-Bayan that Imam al-Sarakhsi said in Kitab al-Jami' that upon him is 'ushr in all cases because it is more entitled to 'ushr than to kharaj, and that is the apparent view. The answer is that what is prohibited is imposing kharaj initially by force; but by choice it is permissible, and he has chosen it here by irrigating with kharaj water. It is like when a person revives dead land with the imam's permission and irrigates it with kharaj water: then kharaj is obligatory on him. And the garden has a wall around it and scattered trees. Thus in al-Mi'raj. It is restricted to making it a garden, because if he did not make it a garden and there are date palms yielding many wasqs, there is nothing on him.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from rational reasoning. The matn states that when a Muslim makes his house into a garden, the tax depends on the water source. The commentator explains: if irrigated with 'ushr water (from rain-fed rivers), it is 'ushri; if with kharaj water (from canals that charge), it becomes kharaji. If mixed equally, 'ushr is dominant because it is more entitled. A problem raised by al-'Utabi is that imposing kharaj on a Muslim initially seems prohibited, but Imam al-Sarakhsi says 'ushr applies in all cases. The commentator resolves that compulsory kharaj is prohibited, but voluntarily choosing to use kharaj water is allowed, similar to reviving dead land with permission and using kharaj water. The restriction 'making it a garden' is because if trees are already present without making it a garden, no tax is due (they are like natural growth).

Key Terms
البستان (al-bustan): A garden or orchard, typically with a wall and scattered trees.
العشر (al-'ushr): A tithe (10% or 5%) on agricultural produce from rain-fed or naturally irrigated land.
الخراج (al-kharaj): A land tax on conquered or kharaji land, typically a fixed annual amount or proportional to produce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Kharaj
B) 'Ushr
C) Half 'ushr and half kharaj
D) No tax is due

Key Takeaway
A Muslim's garden tax follows the water source; voluntary use of kharaj water makes the land kharaji, resolving the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Exemption of Houses from 'Ushr: Ruling and Proof
Ruling statement with proof from Companion practice and ijma'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the exemption of dwellings from 'ushr and identify the evidence from 'Umar's practice and ijma'.

Core Idea
Houses are exempt from 'ushr by the practice of 'Umar and consensus of the Companions.

Zakat on land produce is called 'ushr, but not all land yields are taxable. This passage clarifies that dwellings (houses) are exempt from 'ushr, citing the practice of Caliph 'Umar and the consensus of the Companions. The commentary further expands this ruling to graveyards and uses a reasoning from Al-Hidayah to include houses of all dhimmis. Understanding this exemption helps distinguish taxable agricultural land from non-taxable residential property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The matn states 'wa darahu harr'—his house is free (exempt from 'ushr). Recognize the hukm: exemption (عفواً).
The ruling is mubah in the sense of no tax liability.
2
Locate the proof
The sharh cites the practice of 'Umar and the consensus of the Companions as the basis. Understand that this is an ijma' proof.
Ijma' is a primary source of law.
3
Analyze the expansion from Hidayah
Al-Hidayah restricts the phrase 'his house' to a Zoroastrian, implying that if a magus's house is exempt, then the house of a Jew or Christian is even more exempt. This is an implication (dalalah) based on remoteness.
This is a probe: why specify the magus? To extend the ruling to all dhimmis.
4
Apply to analogous cases
The commentary explicitly adds graveyards as also exempt. Think of other similar properties that are not agricultural, like workshops or storehouses.
The illa is non-agricultural use.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وداره حر) لأن عمر رضي الله عنه جعل المساكن عفواً وعليه إجماع الصحابة وكذا المقابر، وتقييده في الهداية بالمجوسي ليفيد النفي في غيره من أهل الكتاب بالدلالة لأن المجوسي أبعد
His statement: 'and his house is free', because 'Umar radiya Llahu 'anhu made dwellings exempt, and upon that is the consensus of the Companions; likewise graveyards. And its restriction in Al-Hidayah to a Zoroastrian (majusi) indicates the negation for others of the People of the Book by implication, because the Zoroastrian is more remote.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This is a ruling statement with proof of ijma'. The matn says that one's house is free from 'ushr. The commentator explains that this is based on the practice of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab and the consensus of the Companions. This applies also to graveyards. The commentator notes a restriction in Al-Hidayah: it mentions that the house of a Zoroastrian is exempt (for they are even more removed from the law), implying that the houses of other dhimmis (People of the Book) are also exempt, as their status is even stronger. This shows careful Hanafi legal reasoning: if a less privileged class is exempt, the more privileged is certainly exempt.

Key Terms
حر (harr): Free, exempt from tax.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Qiyas
D) Custom

Key Takeaway
Dwellings are exempt from 'ushr based on established proof from 'Umar and ijma'.

Muslim's Garden: Water Source Determines Land Tax
Ruling statement with expansion on water source and enclosure conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on land tax for a Muslim's garden and identify the conditions that determine whether the land is 'ushri or kharaji.

Core Idea
A Muslim's garden is 'ushri or kharaji depending on water source; 'ushr takes precedence when both are used.

When a Muslim converts his residential land into a garden, the land tax classification hinges on the water source used for irrigation. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that if the water is from rain or wells ('ushr water), the land is 'ushri; if from government-maintained canals (kharaj water), it becomes kharaji. The commentary also addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a Muslim can be subject to kharaj at all, resolving that it is permissible by choice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The ruling applies when a Muslim turns his house (dar) into a garden (bustan). Note that the garden must be enclosed and contain trees.
Scattered trees on non-garden land do not trigger the tax.
2
Determine the water source
If irrigated solely with 'ushr water (rain, well, spring, given rivers), the land remains 'ushri. If solely with kharaj water (state canals), it becomes kharaji.
'Ushr water is naturally occurring; kharaj water involves state infrastructure.
3
Apply the rule for mixed sources
If the garden is irrigated with both types, 'ushr takes precedence. The land is 'ushri and only 'ushr is due.
This is because 'ushr is more entitled (ahaqq) than kharaj.
4
Understand the voluntary kharaj
Al-Atabi objected that kharaj on a Muslim is problematic, but al-Sarakhsi explained that it is only prohibited when imposed by compulsion. If the Muslim chooses to use kharaj water, he voluntarily accepts kharaj.
This is analogous to reviving dead land with the Imam's permission.
5
Apply the enclosure condition
The ruling only applies to a garden (bustan) that is enclosed. If the land has date palms but no enclosure, nothing is due even if it yields heavily.
The enclosure (ha'it) is a defining feature of bustan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن جعل مسلم داره بستاناً فمؤنته تدور مائه يعني فإن سقاه بماء العشر فهو عشري، وإن سقاه بماء الخراج فهو خراجي، وإن سقاه مرة من ماء العشر ومرة من ماء الخراج فعليه العشر لأنه أحق بالعشر من الخراج. كذا في غاية البيان. واستشكل العتابي وجوب الخراج على المسلم ابتداء حتى نقل في غاية البيان أن الإمام السرخسي ذكر في كتاب الجامع أن عليه العشر بكل حال لأنه أحق بالعشر من الخراج وهو الأظهر. وجوابه أن الممنوع وضع الخراج عليه ابتداء جبراً، أما باختياره فيجوز وقد اختاره هنا حيث سقاه بماء الخراج فهو كما إذا أحيا أرضاً ميتة بإذن الإمام وسقاها بماء الخراج فإنه يجب عليه الخراج والبستان يحوط عليها حائط وفيها أشجار متفرقة. كذا في المعراج. قيد بجعلها بستاناً لأنه لو لم يجعلها بستاناً وفيها نخل تغل أكراراً لا شيء فيها.
And if a Muslim makes his house a garden, its burden (tax) revolves with its water, meaning if he irrigates it with 'ushr water then it is 'ushri, if he irrigates it with kharaj water then it is kharaji, and if he irrigates it once with 'ushr water and once with kharaj water then he owes 'ushr because it is more entitled to 'ushr than kharaj. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. Al-Atabi raised a difficulty about the obligation of kharaj on a Muslim initially, until it was narrated in Ghayat al-Bayan that Imam al-Sarakhsi mentioned in Kitab al-Jami' that he owes 'ushr in all cases because it is more entitled to 'ushr than kharaj, and that is the most apparent. Its answer: what is prohibited is imposing kharaj on him initially by compulsion; as for by his choice, it is permissible, and he has chosen it here by irrigating with kharaj water, so it is like if he revives a dead land with the Imam's permission and irrigates it with kharaj water, then kharaj is obligatory upon him. And the garden is surrounded by a wall and has scattered trees. Thus in al-Mi'raj. He restricted it to making it a garden because if he does not make it a garden and it has date palms yielding many loads, nothing is due.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It clarifies that when a Muslim converts his residential land into a garden, the tax (whether 'ushr or kharaj) depends on the water source used for irrigation. If he uses rainwater or well water (which is 'ushr water), the land is 'ushri. If he uses water from rivers or canals that are subject to kharaj (i.e., maintained by the state), the land becomes kharaji. If both types are used, 'ushr takes precedence because it is more closely tied to the land's productivity. The commentary also addresses an objection from al-Atabi: normally, imposing kharaj on a Muslim initially is not allowed unless by his own choice. Here, by choosing to irrigate with kharaj water, the Muslim voluntarily accepts kharaj. The garden must be enclosed; if no enclosure and only scattered trees, nothing is due.

Key Terms
ماء العشر (ma' al-ushr): Water from rain, wells, springs, and major rivers not subject to government control.
ماء الخراج (ma' al-kharaj): Water from man-made canals or government-maintained irrigation systems.
البستان (al-bustan): An enclosed garden with trees, as opposed to scattered trees.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a Muslim irrigates his garden once with rain water and once with canal water, what is owed?
A) Only 'ushr because 'ushr is more entitled.
B) Only kharaj because kharaj water was used.
C) Both 'ushr and kharaj proportionally.
D) Nothing because the garden is not enclosed.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim's garden tax is determined by water source; 'ushr prevails when mixed, and voluntary use of kharaj water is permissible.

beginner
6 min
Dhimmi's Residential House Exempt from Kharaj
States a dhimmi's house and graveyards are tax-exempt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the tax status of a dhimmi's residential house and identify the proof (Ijma) and the implied reasoning (fahwa al-khitab) for extending the exemption to all dhimmis.

Core Idea
A dhimmi's residential house and graveyards are exempt from kharaj by the consensus of the Companions.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a key point in Hanafi fiscal law: a dhimmi's dwelling is not subject to land tax. The ruling is rooted in the practice of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab (RA) and the subsequent consensus of the Companions. The commentary also explains why the Hidayah mentions the Zoroastrian specifically—to strengthen the case for other dhimmis by implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is being ruled upon? A dhimmi's residential house (and graveyards). What is the ruling? They are exempt from kharaj (land tax).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the action of 'Umar (RA) making dwellings exempt and the subsequent consensus (ijma) of the Companions on this ruling.
Ijma of the Sahaba is a primary source of law in the Hanafi school.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The consensus is qati' (definitive) in both transmission and meaning, so the ruling is binding. The house is exempt because the Sahaba agreed that dwellings are not subject to kharaj.
4
Understand the expansion (fahwa al-khitab)
The Hidayah mentions the Zoroastrian (majusi) to imply by the more logical reasoning (fahwa) that other dhimmis (People of the Book) are even more entitled to the exemption, since the Zoroastrian is considered further removed from the obligation of 'ushr (which applies to Muslims).
This is an example of textual implication (dalalah al-nass) in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : بخلاف الذمي لأنه أهل له لا للعشر قوله : وداره حر لأن عمر رضي الله عنه جعل المساكن عفواً وعليه إجماع الصحابة وكذا المقابر، وتقييده في الهداية بالمجوسي ليفيد النفي في غيره من أهل الكتاب بالدلالة لأن المجوسي أبعد في وجوب ثلاثة أرباع العشر تأمل
His saying: contrary to the dhimmi because he is entitled to it (kharaj) and not to 'ushr. His saying: his house is exempt. Because 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) made dwellings exempt, and the Companions agreed upon it, and likewise graveyards. And its restriction in al-Hidayah to the Zoroastrian (majusi) serves to indicate the negation for others of the People of the Book by implication, because the Zoroastrian is further removed in the obligation of three-fourths of 'ushr. Consider.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn that states a dhimmi's house is free from tax. The basis is the ruling of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab (RA) that dwellings are exempt, and this was a consensus of the Companions. Graveyards are also exempt. The Hidayah restricts this to Zoroastrians (Majus) to imply by more logical reasoning that other dhimmis (People of the Book) are also exempt, since the Zoroastrian is even further from being subject to 'ushr (which applies to Muslims).

Key Terms
الذمي (adh-dhimmi): A non-Muslim citizen of the Islamic state who pays jizya.
داره حر (dāruhū ḥurr): His house is free/exempt from land tax.
المجوسي (al-majūsī): Zoroastrian, considered a separate category from People of the Book in early fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a dhimmi's house exempt from land tax according to this passage?
A) Because dhimmis already pay jizya.
B) Based on 'Umar's ruling and ijma' of the Companions.
C) Because houses are not agricultural land.
D) Because the matn says so without proof.

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi's residential house and graveyards are exempt from kharaj by the definitive consensus of the Sahaba, and the mention of the Zoroastrian in Al-Hidayah serves to extend this exemption to all dhimmis by stronger implication.

intermediate

Ruling on Qir and Nift Deposits in 'Ushr and Kharaj Lands
Discusses tax liability for qir and nift deposits in agricultural land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qir (pitch) and nift (naphtha) deposits in agricultural land and explain why they are treated like water springs for tax purposes.

Core Idea
A mineral spring like qir is not agricultural produce no 'ushr or kharaj unless arable land behind it.

This lesson examines the tax liability for non-agricultural resources that emerge from land, specifically qir (bitumen) and nift (naphtha). The Hanafi school distinguishes between 'ushri and kharaji lands and clarifies that these deposits are akin to water springs, not crops. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion over mineral taxation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the resource and land type
Determine whether the substance (qir or nift) emerges like a spring and whether the land is 'ushri or kharaji.
The passage treats qir and nift identically as flowing springs.
2
Assess if there is arable land behind the deposit
Check if the deposit site is surrounded by cultivable land. This condition changes the tax ruling.
If no arable land behind, no tax is due on the deposit itself.
3
Apply the ruling for 'ushri land
In 'ushri land, no 'ushr is due because 'ushr requires actual produce, not just ability to cultivate.
Even if arable land exists behind, no 'ushr on the deposit; the arable land is taxed separately.
4
Apply the ruling for kharaji land
In kharaji land, if arable land exists behind, kharaj is due on the entire area because ability to cultivate suffices for kharaj.
The deposit site is considered part of the productive land for kharaj purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : كعين قير ونفط في أرض عشر ولو في أرض خراج يجب الخراج لأنه ليس من إنزال الأرض وإنما هو عين فوارة كعين الماء فلا عشر ولا خراج إن لم يكن وراء موضع القير والنفط أرض فارغة صالحة للزراعة، وأما إذا كان وراءه موضع صالح للزراعة فلا يجب شيء إن كان في أرض العشر لأن العشر لا يكفي فيه التمكن من الزراعة بل لا بد من حقيقة الخارج، وأما إن كان في أرض خراج وجب الخراج لأنه يكفي لوجوبه التمكن من الزراعة وقد حصل وهو المراد بما في المختصر . والقير هو الزفت ويقال القار. والنفط بالفتح والكسر وهو أفصح دهن يعلو الماء وفي معراج الدراية ولا يمسح موضع القير في رواية ابن سماعة عن محمد لأن موضعه لا يصلح للزراعة. وقال بعض مشايخنا : لأن يمسح موضع القير تبع للأرض فيمسح معه تبعاً يصلح للزراعة كأرض في بعض جوانبها سبخة فإنها تمسح مع الأرض ويوضع الخراج فيها لكونها تابعة لما يصلح للزراعة ا هـ . وظاهر المختصر يدل على قول البعض فإنه أوجب الخراج مطلقاً، ولم يذكر المصنف الفرق بين الأرض الخراجية والعشرية فالأرض العشرية أرض العرب كلها قال محمد : هي من العذيب إلى مكة وعدن أبين إلى أقصى حجر باليمن بمهرة. وذكر الكرخي أنها أرض الحجاز وتهامة واليمن ومكة والطائف والبرية، ومنها الأرض التي أسلم أهلها طوعاً أو فتحت قهراً وقسمت بين الغانمين. وأما الأرض الخراجية فما فتحت قهراً وتركت في أيدي أربابها وأرض نصارى بني تغلب . والموات التي أحياها ذمي مطلقاً أو مسلم وسقاها بماء الخراج، وماء الخراج هو ماء الأنهار الصغار التي حفرها الأعاجم مما يدخل تحت الأيدي وماء العيون والقنوات المستنبطة من مال بيت المال. وماء العشر هو ماء السماء والآبار والعيون والأنهار العظام التي لا تدخل تحت الأيدي كسيحون وجيحون ودجلة والفرات والنيل لعدم إثبات يد عليها وعن أبي يوسف أنها خراجية لإمكان إثبات اليد عليها بشد السفن بعضها على بعض حتى تصير شبه القنطرة. كذا في البدائع وغيرها والله أعلم. حاصل الجواب تسليم أن وضع الخراج على المسلم ابتداء جائز لكن لا مطلقاً، بل إذا كان برضاه وأن الممنوع وضعه عليه جبراً وأجاب في الفتح بما حاصله أن هذا ليس فيه وضع الخراج عليه ابتداء أصلاً وإنما هو انتقال ما وظيفته الخراج إليه بوظيفته وهو الماء فإن وظيفته الخراج، فإذا سقي به انتقل هو بوظيفته إلى أرض المسلم كما لو اشتري خراجية . قوله : ( وعدن أبين قال في القاموس : وعدن أبين محركة جزيرة باليمن أقام بها أبين .
His saying: like a spring of qir (pitch) and nift (naphtha) in 'ushr land, and if in kharaj land, kharaj is obligatory. Because it is not a product of the land but rather a flowing spring like a water spring, so neither 'ushr nor kharaj is due if there is no empty land behind the qir and nift site suitable for cultivation. But if there is a site suitable for cultivation behind it, then nothing is due if it is in 'ushr land, because 'ushr does not suffice with mere ability to cultivate; rather, actual produce is necessary. But if it is in kharaj land, kharaj is obligatory because ability to cultivate suffices for its obligation, and that has occurred. This is the intended meaning in al-Mukhtasar. Qir is asphalt, also called qar. Nift is pronounced with fatha or kasra; it is the more eloquent term for oil that floats on water. In Ma'raj al-Diraya: the site of qir is not measured in the estimate of land in one narration from Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad, because its site is not suitable for cultivation. And some of our shaykhs said: rather, the site of qir is measured as part of the land, so it is measured along with it as a subsidiary because it is suitable for cultivation, like land in some edges of which is saline marsh; then it is measured with the land and kharaj is imposed on it because it is subsidiary to what is suitable for cultivation. The apparent meaning of al-Mukhtasar supports the latter opinion, as it obligates kharaj absolutely. And the author did not mention the difference between kharaji and 'ushri land. So 'ushri land is all the land of the Arabs. Muhammad said: it is from al-'Udhayb to Makkah and 'Adan Abyan to the furthest Hajr in Yemen at Mahra. And al-Karkhi mentioned that it is the land of Hijaz, Tihama, Yemen, Makkah, Ta'if, and the wilderness, and among it is land whose people embraced Islam voluntarily or were conquered by force and divided among the warriors. As for kharaji land, it is what was conquered by force and left in the hands of its owners, and the land of the Christians of Banu Taghlib. And dead lands that a dhimmi revived absolutely, or a Muslim revived and irrigated with kharaj water. Kharaj water is the water of small rivers dug by non-Arabs that come under control, and the water of springs and canals excavated from the public treasury. 'Ushr water is the water of rain, wells, springs, and the great rivers that do not come under control, like Sayhun, Jayhun, Dajla, Furat, Nile, due to the impossibility of establishing control. And from Abu Yusuf: they are kharaji because it is possible to establish control by tying ships together to form a bridge. Thus in al-Bada'i' and others. And Allah knows best. The summary of the answer: conceding that imposing kharaj on a Muslim initially is permissible, but not absolutely; rather, when it is with his consent, and what is prohibited is imposing it by compulsion. And he answered in al-Fath with what amounts to: this is not imposing kharaj on him initially at all; rather, it is a transfer of what is its function (kharaj) to him with its function, which is water; for its function is kharaj. So if he irrigates with it, it transfers with its function to the Muslim's land, just as if he bought a kharaji land. His saying: (and 'Adan Abyan) said in al-Qamus: 'Adan Abyan, with a voweled letter, is a peninsula in Yemen where Abyan resided.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This lengthy passage is a commentary on a matn ruling about qir (bitumen) and nift (oil) found in agricultural land. It begins by stating that if such a resource emerges like a flowing spring, it is not considered produce of the land, so no 'ushr or kharaj is due unless there is arable land behind the deposit. If there is arable land, then in 'ushri land, no tax because 'ushr requires actual produce; but in kharaji land, kharaj is due because ability to cultivate triggers it. There is an ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school about whether the site of the deposit is measured as part of the land for tax purposes. One narration from Imam Muhammad says it is not measured because it is unsuitable for agriculture; some later scholars say it is measured as a subsidiary. The apparent meaning of al-Mukhtasar supports the latter. Then the passage defines 'ushri and kharaji lands, describing the boundaries of 'ushri lands according to Muhammad and al-Karkhi, and definitions of kharaji land. It also defines 'ushr water and kharaj water, with a note from Abu Yusuf that all great rivers could be considered kharaji if control is possible. Finally, there is a summary answer to an earlier objection about voluntary acceptance of kharaj by a Muslim, concluding that using kharaj water transfers the function of kharaj to the land.

Key Terms
القير (al-qir): Bitumen or asphalt, a mineral substance that may seep from the ground.
النفط (an-naft): Naphtha or petroleum, an oil that floats on water.
المختصر (al-Mukhtasar): A reference to a Hanafi fiqh work (likely al-Mukhtasar by al-Quduri).
الأرض العشرية (al-ard al-'ushriyya): Land subject to 'ushr (tithe) tax, typically owned by Muslims and watered by natural sources.
الأرض الخراجية (al-ard al-kharajiyya): Land subject to kharaj (land tax), typically conquered land left in original owners' hands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In 'ushri land, if a qir spring appears but behind it there is arable land, what is the tax liability?
A) 'Ushr is due on the qir because it is produce.
B) Kharaj is due on the arable land behind it.
C) Nothing is due because 'ushr requires actual produce, not just ability to cultivate.
D) Both 'ushr and kharaj are due proportionally.

Key Takeaway
Qir and nift are not agricultural products; they are treated like water springs, so no 'ushr or kharaj is imposed on them unless they are on arable land in kharaji territory.

intermediate
6 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Measuring the Deposit Site for Tax Assessment
Presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on measuring the deposit site

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi positions on whether the site of a qir deposit is measured as part of the land for tax assessment and evaluate which view aligns with al-Mukhtasar.

Core Idea
Scholars differ: should the barren deposit site be measured with the arable land for kharaj, or omitted? The answer changes the tax base.

This lesson delves into an internal Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding land measurement for kharaj. When a qir or nift deposit occupies part of a plot, should that area be included in the taxable land? The source presents two narrations: one from Imam Muhammad via Ibn Sama'a that the site is not measured, and another from later Hanafi shaykhs that it is measured as a subsidiary, like a saline patch. The apparent meaning of al-Mukhtasar supports the latter view, though the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq does not give a definitive tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the point of dispute
The dispute is whether the physical area occupied by the qir or nift deposit should be included in the land measurement for assessing kharaj.
This affects the total taxable area and thus the tax amount.
2
State the first position (Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad)
According to this narration, the site of qir is not measured because it is not suitable for cultivation.
This view treats the deposit as an extraneous feature that does not contribute to agricultural potential.
3
State the second position (later shaykhs)
The later shaykhs argue that the site is measured as part of the land because it is subsidiary to the surrounding arable land, analogous to a saline marsh within a field.
Subsidiary parts of a plot are included in the assessment even if individually unproductive.
4
Analyze the evidence and tarjih
The author notes that the apparent meaning of al-Mukhtasar supports the second position because it obligates kharaj absolutely, without exception for the deposit site. However, no strong tarjih is given; both views are noted.
The passage does not resolve the dispute conclusively, but the reference to al-Mukhtasar tips the scale toward the inclusion view.

If there is arable land, then in 'ushri land, no tax because 'ushr requires actual produce; but in kharaji land, kharaj is due because ability to cultivate triggers it. There is an ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school about whether the site of the deposit is measured as part of the land for tax purposes. One narration from Imam Muhammad says it is not measured because it is unsuitable for agriculture; some later scholars say it is measured as a subsidiary. The apparent meaning of al-Mukhtasar supports the latter. Then the passage defines 'ushri and kharaji lands, describing the boundaries of 'ushri lands according to Muhammad and al-Karkhi, and definitions of kharaji land. It also defines 'ushr water and kharaj water, with a note from Abu Yusuf that all great rivers could be considered kharaji if control is possible. Finally, there is a summary answer to an earlier objection about voluntary acceptance of kharaj by a Muslim, concluding that using kharaj water transfers the function of kharaj to the land.

Key Terms
القير (al-qir): Bitumen or asphalt, a mineral substance that may seep from the ground.
النفط (an-naft): Naphtha or petroleum, an oil that floats on water.
المختصر (al-Mukhtasar): A reference to a Hanafi fiqh work (likely al-Mukhtasar by al-Quduri).
الأرض العشرية (al-ard al-'ushriyya): Land subject to 'ushr (tithe) tax, typically owned by Muslims and watered by natural sources.
الأرض الخراجية (al-ard al-kharajiyya): Land subject to kharaj (land tax), typically conquered land left in original owners' hands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is measured with the land.
B) It is not measured.
C) It is measured only if it has a well.
D) It is taxed separately.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has two views on measuring the deposit site; the later shaykhs' view of inclusion aligns with al-Mukhtasar, but the issue remains open.

Ruling on Tar and Naphtha Springs in 'Ushri and Kharaji Lands
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kharaj assessment for oil/tar springs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on tar/naphtha springs in 'ushri vs. kharaji land and identify the relied-upon position in the intra-Hanafi dispute over measuring the spring's site.

Core Idea
A non-cultivable spring in kharaji land incurs kharaj if cultivable land surrounds it; in 'ushri land, no 'ushr is due.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the treatment of oil and tar springs in agricultural tax law. The key distinction hinges on the type of land and the presence of cultivable area around the spring. We will also examine an intra-Hanafi disagreement about whether the spring's site should be measured for kharaj assessment, and conclude with the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the land type
Determine whether the land is 'ushri (tithe land) or kharaji (land tax land). The passage defines 'ushri land as the Arabian Peninsula and lands whose inhabitants accepted Islam voluntarily or were conquered and distributed. Kharaji land is conquered land left in the hands of its owners, and land of Banu Taghlib Christians.
'Ushri and kharaji status affects the basis of taxation: 'ushr requires actual produce; kharaj requires only potential cultivation.
2
Assess cultivable land behind the spring
If there is a tar/naphtha spring, look for arable land behind it (i.e., surrounding it). If no cultivable land exists, neither 'ushr nor kharaj is due on the spring itself, as it is like a water spring.
The spring's site itself is not cultivable, so the ruling depends on the surrounding land.
3
Apply the ruling based on land type
If there IS cultivable land behind the spring: in 'ushri land, no 'ushr is owed because 'ushr requires actual harvest, not mere possibility. In kharaji land, kharaj IS owed because for kharaj, the possibility of cultivation suffices.
This is the primary ruling from the Mukhtasar and the relied-upon position.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Ibn Sama'ah reports from Imam Muhammad that the spring's site itself is not measured for kharaj because it is not cultivable. Some later mashayikh (some scholars) argue that the site is measured as part of the land, analogous to a salty patch within arable land. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq sides with the mashayikh, as does the Mukhtasar.
The practical effect: if the site is not measured, the kharaj is only on the arable portion; if measured, the entire area including spring site is taxed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في أرض عشر ولو في أرض خراج يجب الخراج.
وإن كان
كتاب الزكاة باب العشر
عن الإسلام لحرمة مناكحته وذبائحه قوله : (كعين قير ونفط في أرض عشر ولو في أرض خراج يجب الخراج) لأنه ليس من إنزال الأرض وإنما هو عين فوارة كعين الماء فلا عشر ولا خراج إن لم يكن وراء موضع القير والنفط أرض فارغة صالحة للزراعة، وأما إذا كان وراءه موضع صالح للزراعة فلا يجب شيء إن كان في أرض العشر لأن العشر لا يكفي فيه التمكن من الزراعة بل لا بد من حقيقة الخارج، وأما إن كان في أرض خراج وجب الخراج لأنه يكفي لوجوبه التمكن من الزراعة وقد حصل وهو المراد بما في المختصر . والقير هو الزفت ويقال القار. والنفط بالفتح والكسر وهو أفصح دهن يعلو الماء وفي معراج الدراية ولا يمسح موضع القير في رواية ابن سماعة عن محمد لأن موضعه لا يصلح للزراعة. وقال بعض مشايخنا : لأن يمسح موضع القير تبع للأرض فيمسح معه تبعاً يصلح للزراعة كأرض في بعض جوانبها سبخة فإنها تمسح مع الأرض ويوضع الخراج فيها لكونها تابعة لما يصلح للزراعة ا هـ . وظاهر المختصر يدل على قول البعض فإنه أوجب الخراج مطلقاً، ولم يذكر المصنف الفرق بين الأرض الخراجية والعشرية فالأرض العشرية أرض العرب كلها قال محمد : هي من العذيب إلى مكة وعدن أبين إلى أقصى حجر باليمن بمهرة. وذكر الكرخي أنها أرض الحجاز وتهامة واليمن ومكة والطائف والبرية، ومنها الأرض التي أسلم أهلها طوعاً أو فتحت قهراً وقسمت بين الغانمين. وأما الأرض الخراجية فما فتحت قهراً وتركت في أيدي أربابها وأرض نصارى بني تغلب . والموات التي أحياها ذمي مطلقاً أو مسلم وسقاها بماء الخراج، وماء الخراج هو ماء الأنهار الصغار التي حفرها الأعاجم مما يدخل تحت الأيدي وماء العيون والقنوات المستنبطة من مال بيت المال. وماء العشر هو ماء السماء والآبار والعيون والأنهار العظام التي لا تدخل تحت الأيدي كسيحون وجيحون ودجلة والفرات والنيل لعدم إثبات يد عليها وعن أبي يوسف أنها خراجية لإمكان إثبات اليد عليها بشد السفن بعضها على بعض حتى تصير شبه القنطرة. كذا في البدائع وغيرها والله أعلم.
حاصل الجواب تسليم أن وضع الخراج على المسلم ابتداء جائز لكن لا مطلقاً، بل إذا كان برضاه وأن الممنوع وضعه عليه جبراً وأجاب في الفتح بما حاصله أن هذا ليس فيه وضع الخراج عليه ابتداء أصلاً وإنما هو انتقال ما وظيفته الخراج إليه بوظيفته وهو الماء فإن وظيفته الخراج، فإذا سقي به انتقل هو بوظيفته إلى أرض المسلم كما لو اشتري خراجية .
قوله : ( وعدن أبين قال في القاموس : وعدن أبين محركة جزيرة باليمن أقام بها أبين .
In 'ushr land, and if in kharaj land, kharaj is obligatory. ... The summary of the answer is that imposing kharaj on a Muslim initially is permissible, but not absolutely; only with his consent, and it is prohibited to impose it by force. ... He said: (and 'Adan Abin): in al-Qamus: 'Adan Abin, with vowel marks, is an island in Yemen where Abin resided.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the ruling on tar and naphtha springs in agricultural land. The author first states the ruling: if the spring is in 'ushr land and there is no cultivable land behind it, neither 'ushr nor kharaj is owed; if there is cultivable land behind it, in 'ushr land nothing is owed because 'ushr requires actual produce, while in kharaj land kharaj is owed because mere possibility of cultivation suffices. Then two transmissions from Muhammad are mentioned: one from Ibn Sama'ah that the site of the spring is not measured (for kharaj), and another from some mashayikh that it is measured as part of the land. The author favors the latter view, as does the Mukhtasar. The passage then defines 'ushri and kharaji lands and discusses water rights.

Key Terms
قير (qīr): Tar or pitch, a black viscous substance derived from petroleum.
نفط (nafṭ): Naphtha, a thin oil that floats on water.
أرض عشرية (arḍ ʿushriyya): Land on which 'ushr (tithe) is due, typically land of the Arabs or land whose inhabitants accepted Islam voluntarily or was conquered and distributed among the fighters.
أرض خراجية (arḍ kharājiyya): Land on which kharaj (land tax) is due, typically land conquered by force and left in the hands of its original owners, or land of Christians of Banu Taghlib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a kharaji land, a naphtha spring emerges in a spot that is not cultivable but is surrounded by cultivable fields. What is the ruling according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) No tax is due because the site is not cultivable.
B) Kharaj is obligatory because the site is part of the land and follows its ruling.
C) 'Ushr is obligatory because the spring benefits the land.
D) The ruling depends on whether the spring was created naturally or by humans.

Key Takeaway
A non-cultivable feature in arable kharaji land is subject to kharaj because kharaj attaches to the land's potential, not just its actual cultivation.

Faqir and Miskin: Distinction and Intra-Hanafi Dispute in Non-Zakat Contexts
Defining faqir and miskin and resolving the intra-Hanafi dispute on their status in bequests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of faqir and miskin, explain why the conjunction in the zakat verse implies they are two categories, and apply the difference to bequests.

Core Idea
Faqir and miskin are two categories in zakat; their distinction extends to bequests according to Abu Hanifa.

This lesson examines the foundational distinction between faqir (poor) and miskin (needy) in the Hanafi school. While it is settled that they are two separate categories for zakat, a key intra-Hanafi dispute arises in non-zakat contexts such as bequests. We will explore the evidence for each view and the practical financial consequences.

Guided Path
1
Define the Terms
Identify the Hanafi definitions of faqir and miskin from the passage. Note that miskin is worse off (has nothing), while faqir has less than nisab but something.
This is the established view in the school.
2
Locate the Proof for Two Categories in Zakat
Find the Quranic evidence: the conjunction 'wa' in 'li'l-fuqara'i wa'l-masakin' implies difference (mughayara). No one disputes they are two categories for zakat.
This proof is decisive for zakat.
3
Identify the Dispute
Recognize that the dispute (Imam Abu Hanifa vs. Imam Abu Yusuf) is whether they remain two categories in non-zakat contexts like bequests, endowments, and vows.
Abu Hanifa says two; Abu Yusuf says one.
4
Examine the Proofs
Abu Hanifa relies on the literal conjunction and the fact that bequests are not need-based. Abu Yusuf relies on common usage and the idea that zakat's rationale (need) is absent.
The passage provides the Hanafi response: the literal wording must be followed.
5
Apply to a Bequest Scenario
Work through the example: a person bequeaths one-third to a specific person and to the poor and needy. Calculate shares under both views and note the practical difference.
This shows the thamarah al-khilaf (consequence of the difference).

باب المصرف هو الفقير والمسكين وهو أسوأ حالاً من الفقير والعامل والمكاتب والمديون. ولم يقيده في الكتاب بمصرف الزكاة ليتناول الزكاة والعشر وخمس المعادن مما قدمه كما أشير إليه في النهاية. وينبغي إخراج خمس المعادن لأن مصرفه الغنائم . كما صرح به الأسبيجابي وغيره. وقد ذكر الأصناف السبعة وسكت عن المؤلفة قلوبهم للإشارة إلى السقوط للإجماع الصحابي وهو من قبيل انتهاء الحكم لانتهاء علته الغائبة التي كان والمسكين من لا شيء له، وقيل على العكس ولكل وجه والأول هو الأصح وهو المذهب. كذا في الكافي. والأولى أن يفسر له ما دون النصاب كما في النقاية أخذاً من قولهم يجوز دفع الزكاة إلى من يملك ما دون النصاب أو
