Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate
10 min
Definition of 'Fi Sabilillah' in Zakat: The Stranded Fighter
Explaining the meaning of 'fi sabilillah' in the zakat verse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'fi sabilillah' as a zakat category and distinguish the rulings of Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and al-Bada'i.

Core Idea
The stranded fighter is the primary meaning of 'fi sabilillah' in zakat, but the category expands to all needy acts of obedience.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the meaning of the zakat category 'fi sabilillah' by identifying the stranded fighter as the intended recipient. The commentator follows Abu Yusuf's view and also records the interpretations of Muhammad al-Shaybani and the broader expansion by al-Bada'i. We will examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and the essential condition of poverty that governs all interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its legal context
Locate the phrase 'fi sabilillah' in the zakat verse (Surah al-Tawbah 9:60). Understand that it is the eighth category of zakat recipients.
The general phrase requires specification.
2
State the primary Hanafi specification
According to the commentator, the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq chose Abu Yusuf's view: 'fi sabilillah' refers to the stranded fighter (munqati' al-ghuzat).
This is the phrase 'منقطع الغزاة'.
3
Note the alternative Hanafi views
Muhammad al-Shaybani says it is the stranded pilgrim. Others say students of knowledge. Al-Bada'i expands it to all acts of obedience.
All require the condition of poverty.
4
Identify the universal condition
The passage stresses that poverty (al-ihityaj) is necessary on all interpretations. Without need, the category does not apply.
This is a key condition for validity.
5
Determine the practical difference
Since poverty is already a condition for all zakat recipients, the benefit of these different interpretations appears in wills and endowments, not in zakat itself.
The commentator mentions this explicitly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومنقطع الغزاة) هو المراد بقوله تعالى ﴿وفي سبيل الله﴾ [التوبة : ٦٠] وهو اختيار منه لقول أبي يوسف، وعند محمد منقطع الحاج ، وقيل : طلبة العلم، واقتصر عليه في الفتاوي الظهيرية وفسره في البدائع بجميع القرب فيدخل فيه كل من سعى في طاعة الله تعالى وسبيل الخيرات إذا كان محتاجاً ا.هـ. ولا يخفى أن قيد الفقير لا بد منه على الوجوه كلها فحينئذ لا تظهر ثمرته في الزكاة وإنما تظهر في الوصايا والأوقاف كما تقدم نظيره في الفقراء والمساكين
His statement: (and the stranded fighter) he is the intended meaning of His statement the Exalted 'and in the path of Allah' [9:60]. And this is his choice in agreement with Abu Yusuf's statement. According to Muhammad, it is the stranded pilgrim. And it is said: it is the students of knowledge. And al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah restricted it to that, and al-Bada'i interpreted it as all acts of obedience, so it includes everyone who strives in obedience to Allah and the path of good if he is needy. End. And it is not hidden that the condition of poverty is necessary in all aspects; so then its benefit does not appear in zakat, but rather it appears in wills and endowments, as its parallel was mentioned earlier concerning the poor and the needy.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage discusses the category of 'munqati' al-ghuzat' (the stranded fighter) as the intended meaning of 'fi sabilillah' in the zakat verse. The commentator notes that the author (of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) chose the interpretation of Abu Yusuf, that it refers to a fighter stranded from his wealth. Muhammad al-Shaybani says it refers to a stranded pilgrim. Others say it includes students of knowledge. al-Bada'i expands it to all good deeds. However, the condition of poverty is essential for all these interpretations. The practical difference does not appear in zakat (since all must be poor) but in wills and endowments, where a non-poor person can benefit if specified.

Key Terms
منقطع الغزاة (munqati' al-ghuzat): A fighter who is stranded from his wealth and needs assistance to continue or return.
في سبيل الله (fi sabilillah): In the path of Allah; a category of zakat recipients, interpreted as stranded fighters or all good deeds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, what does 'fi sabilillah' mean in the context of zakat recipients?
A) All charitable projects
B) The stranded fighter
C) The stranded pilgrim
D) Students of knowledge

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets 'fi sabilillah' in zakat primarily as the stranded fighter, but expands to all needy individuals striving in obedience, with poverty as an essential condition.
